responsible for supervision and coordination of project activities; it
would support implementation of all project components in the areas of
procurement, financial administration, documentation, monitoring and
reporting. The current small staff (i.e., director, administrator and
support staff), would be strengthened by an architect (for the first three
years of the project) to assist in implementation of hardware components,



- 21 -
and by an accountant. The project would finance: (a) furniture and
equipment; (b) 85 man-months of specialist services (36 months for project
architect, 30 months of short-term consultant services for pre-investment
studies (including any studies/experimentation required to introduce
multi-grade teaching, cf. para. 3.12), and 19 months for preparation of
bidding documents, evaluation of project achievements and for project
audit); and (c) operating costs. At negotiations, the Government gave
assurances that the PCU will be staffed as described above.
B.   Proiect Cost and Financing Plan
3.37      The total project costs over the five years of implementation is
estimated at US$37.4 million equivalent (net of taxes and duties) with a
foreign exchange component of US$ 28.5 million (76Z). Base cost estimates
are at January 1988 prices. The project would be financed by IDA (US$18.2
million or 49Z of total costs), AfDB (US$17.0 million or 45Z), contri-
butions by local communities to primary school construction and by the
private sector to operational costs of vocational training (US$1.6 million
or 42), and budgetary allocations from the Government (US$0.7 million or
2X). The AfDB would finance printing of textbooks for primary education
(paras. 3.8-3.9), and all investment costs related to science teaching in
secondary and higher education (paras. 3.16-3.20), technical education
(paras. 3.21-3.26), and vocational programs for the industrial fishing
sector (paras. 3.29-3.31). The major assumptions in arriving at the cost
estimates, as well as the detailed project costs by component, cost
category and source of financing, are given in Annex 3-14.
3.38      Recurrent cost implications.  Project investments would generate
net incremental operating costs estimated at about US$6 million over the
five-year implementation period; expressed as a percentage of the 1988
education budget, the costs range from 1.4Z in 1989 to 4.3Z in 1993.
Salaries for teachers for the primary schools to be built account for 48X
of these costs; the remainder is accounted for by increased recurrent costs
in technical education (112) and in vocational programs for the fishing
sector (41Z). The sectoral adiustment program (para.3.2) is estimated to
generate cost savings of about US$20 million over the five-year
implementation of the project; these savings would roughly equal the
additional recurrent costs implied by the adjustment measure aimed at
increasing the budget for primary education by 92 per year through FY1993.
In summary, incremental recurrent costs generated by the project's
investments and adjustment program are comparatively low, especially when
compared to the many benefits the project is expected to generate. Annex
3-15 analyzes the cost savings and incremental recurrent costs generated by
the project.
C.   Proiect Implementation
1.   Proiect Readiness. Management and Institution Building
3.39      Project Readiness.  The Government has provided IDA with a policy
statement containing the Council of Ministers' approval of the Sectoral
Adjustment Program (para. 3.3). Implementation plans and schedules have
been prepared for each component and are found in the Annexes. Suitable
sites for all classrooms, schools and office buildings to be constructed
have been identified, and a survey of all construction activities has been
completed (civil works under the project are summarized in Annex 3-18).



- 22 -
The Government, assisted by UNESCO, is finalizing a prototype of a primary
school classroom. Final site selection for the annual school construction
program will be reviewed by IDA each year. Furniture, pedagogical material
and equipment requirements have been reviewed by IDA and found acceptable.
Most construction, furniture and equipment designs/specifications, and bid
documents would be available at the time of credit effectiveness.
Selection of key experts and training institutions were discussed at
negoti&tions. The Government has already transferred the ENFACOS from the
Ministry of Labor to the MEN (para. 3.24). At negotiations, the Government
agreed with IDA on the items listed in paras. 3.7 and 3.10, and provide IDA
with a signed copy of the contract mentioned in para. 3.31. The cross-
effectiveness condition for the AfDB credit is not expected to cause any
delays as the AfDB intends to submit the project to its Board in May 1988.
3.40      Implementation responsibility and schedule.  Implementation
details of each component are given in Annexes 3-1 through 3-13.
Implementation responsibility for each component rests with the relevant
Ministry Directorate. The Project Coordination Unit (PCU) would act as
liaison with IDA Cor all matters related to project execution and would
support the directorates in the areas of procurement, financial
administration, documentation, monitoring, reporting and preparation of
annual reviews. Project execution has been cautiously planned over a
five-year period. Annex 3-16 describes the PCU and indicates
implementation responsibility ior each project component. At negotiations,
the Government gave assurances that the project would be implemented
according to the arrangements and schedules agreed to at appraisal and
summarized in Annex 3-17.
3.41      Specialist services and training.  The project would provide a
total of 151 man-years of specialist services (of which 70% by local
staff), and a total of 2,155 man-years of training (of which 93% locally
arranged). During negotiations, terms of reference for the specialist
posts would be agreed upon. A condition of effectiveness of the IDA credit
would be that an education planner has been employed in the DPC and that an
architect and an accountant have been employed in the PCU. Details
regarding specialist services and fellowships are given in Annexes 3-19 and
3-20.

3.43      Goods, including pedagogical materials, guides and textbooks
(totalling US$2.58 million equivalent), representing about 682 of the total
vasue of goods financed by IDA, would be procured through international
coi.petitive bidding (ICB) in accordance with IDA guidelines and grouped, to
the extent practical to facilitate competition in bidding, into bid
packages of at least US$150,000 equivalent. 'Where ICB procedures are used,
domestic manufacturers would be given a preference margin, in accordance
with IDA guidelines, of 152 for goods manufactured locally, or the
applicable customs duty, whichever is less. Other contracts for civil
works and school furniture financed by IDA (totalling US$5.9 million
equivalent), which are small in size, labor intensive, involve widely
dispersed sites, and are not likely to attract foreign bidders, would be
awarded through competitive bidding advertised locally (LCB) in accordance
with procedures acceptable to IDA, provided that: (a) all bids shall be
opened in public and bidders' representatives shall be allowed to be
present; (b) no preference margin shall be granted to domestic
manufacturers or suppliers; and (c) foreign companies shall not be required
to be incorporated in Mauritania in order to participate in the bidding.
Items purchased under the category of operating costs which cannot be
grouped into bid packages of at least US$15,000 equivalent, provided the
aggregate amount of such procurement does not exceed US$4 million
equivalent, would be procured through international and local shopping on
the basis of price quotations obtained from at least three foreign or local
suppliers to ensure competitive prices, in accordance with procedures
acceptable to IDA. Specialist services and training abroad financed by IDA
(totalling US$5.3 million equivalent) would be procured in accordance with
IDA guidelines. Bidding packages (announcement, bid documents and proposed
contract awards) estimated at more than US$150,000 equivalent, representing
about 772 of the total value of IDA-financed contracts, would be suoject to
prior review by IDA. The remaining 232 of contracts would be subject to
either: (i) post review for contracts estimated at between US$15,000 and
US$150,000 equivalent; or (ii) random review by IDA supervision missions
for contracts estimated at less than US$15,000 equivalent. Procurement of
contracts for components financed by the AfDB on a parallel basis
(totalling US$17 million equivalent) would follow procedures acceptable to
the AfDB. During negotiations, the Government would give assurances that
it will apply the procurement procedures and arrangements outlined above.
Procurement arrangements are detailed in Annex 3-21.
3.44      Disbursement.  The regional disbursement profile is eight years.
However, in view of the fact that an experienced Project Coordination Unit
is already in place and that most IDA-financed activities are scheduled to
be completed during the first three years of th'.e project, the IDA credit
would be disbursed over a period of five and a hal.f years on th= oasis of
the categories shown in the table below. The project is expected to be
completed by December 31, 1993. The estimated quarterly disbursement
schedule is shown in Annex 3-22, while Annex 3-23 provides a summary of
national contributions to the project.

3.45      Snecial Account.  To facilitate disbursement, US$1.2 million
equivalent, denominated in convertible ouguiya, would be advanced from the
IDA credit and deposited in a local commercial bank. The amount is
estimated to cover about three months of expenses for all IDA-eligible
project expenditures. Replenishment applications to IDA would be
aggregated into packages of at least US$100,000 equivalent and would be
fully documented except for expenses related to contracts and/or
expenditures valued at less than US$15,000 equivalent, which would be
reimbursed against certified statements of expenditures (SOEs), and for
which documentation would be retained at the Project Unit for random review
by IDA supervision missions and for semi-annual audits. Reimbursement
applications would also include a statement of account movements since the
last application, with the balance certified by the bank holding the
account, and a reconciliation showing that the balance represented is the
original amount less payments await'ng reimbursement of small working
advances. Given the availability of the Special Account, the minimum
application for direct payments would normally be for the equivalent of
US$20,000.
3.46      Accounting. auditing and reporting.  The Project Director would
maintain separate accounts for the IDA credit, the AfDB credit and
Government counterpart financing. These accounts would be further
subdivided into expenditures incurred for each component. At negotiations,
the Government gave assurances that: (a) all project accounts, including
disbursement against statements of expenditures (SOEs), will be audited
annually by an independent auditor acceptable to IDA; (b) the Government
will submit the reports to IDA for review by March 31 of each year,
beginning in 1990; (c) the audit reports will certify that the funds were
used for the purposes for which they were provided; and (d) the Project
Director will submit to IDA semi-annual reports on project implementation
and expenditures by June 30 and December 31 of each year, beginning in
1989, and a completion report within six months of the IDA credit closing
date.
IV. PROJECT BENEFITS AND RISKS
4.1       Benefits.  The sectoral adjustment program would help reallocate
education resources in favor of primary education, thus ensuring an



- 26 -
adequate level of investment in basic education during this period of
overall economic adjustment, and a redistribution in favor of the less
well-off social groups of the benefits generated by public spending on
education (between school years 1986/87 and 1993/94, primary education's
share of the education budget would increase from 32Z to 44S, allowing the
enrollment ratio to increase from 55t to 742, and the share of female in
total enrollment from 402 to 442). Together, the project's investments and
adjustment measures would help increase the social returns to all levels of
education by improving educational and skill levels of the labor force,
thereby increasing its contribution to growth in the fishing and service
industries, and improving its mastery of the technologies needed to exploit
the country's meager agricultural and water resources.
4.2       Most students would benefit from the project through more
relevant programs, improved availability, relevance and quality of
pedagogical materials and equipment, better trained teachers and improved
school buildings. Better quality primary education would increase the
utility of this level of schooling to those who cannot continue and would
lead to greater efficiency at subsequent levels. The restructuration,
diversification and quality improvement of technical and vocational
education would help reorient enrollment in line with skill shortages in
potential growth sectors in the economy. Improved quality of science
teaching in secondary and higher education would help correct the current
literary bias in general education, while consolidation of the higher
education sub-sector would help develop institutions that are economically
and pedagogically viable.
4.3       Risks.  The main risks area  (a) the Government's ability to
contain growth in general secondary and higher education and hence to
provide the budgetary resources required to achieve the targets for growth
and quality improvements of primary education; and (b) the capacity of the
economy to generate gainful employment for technical/vocation education
graduates. To address the former risk, the sectoral adjustment program
includes specific measures to control growth in enrollment and expenditures
in general secondary and higher education; implementation of these measures
would be monitored through joint Government/IDA annual reviews.  To
minimize the latter risk, investments are directed towards sectors where
there are demonstrated skill shortages, programs would be kept flexible to
respond to changes in labor market trends, and employers would participate
in the design and financing of training programs. Also, the reinforcement
of the Government's capacity to plan and monitor the development of the
country's human resources would help minimize these risks.
V. AGREEMENTS REACHED AND RECOMMENDATIONS
5.1       Prior to negotiations, the Governments  (a) provided IDA with a
policy statement containing the Council of Ministers' approval of the
Sectoral Adjustment Program (para. 3.3); (b) transferred, from the Ministry
of Labor to the Ministry of Education, the buildings of the former National
School for Administrative, Commercial and Social Training (ENFACOS) to
house the new commercial Lycee (para. 3.24); (c) identified suitable sites
for all classrooms, schools, and office facilities to be built under the
project; and (d) reviewed with IDA the furniture, pedagogical material and
equipment to be procured (para. 3.39).



- 27 -
5.2       During negotiations, the Government and IDA agreed on:
(a) criteria for the location of the classrooms to be built during the
first year of the construction program (para. 3.7); (b) the regulations
governing sales of textbooks and the use of the revolving fund resulting
from such sales (para. 3.10); and tc) terms of reference for the specia >1t
posts (para. 3.41).
5.3       During negotiations, the Government gave assurances that it will
(a) by October 30 of each year, starting in 1989s (i) organize an
annual meeting with IDA to review progress in achieving project
objectives, including those of the sectoral adjustment program
(para. 3.3); and (ii) furnish to IDA (a) the draft recurrent
budget for education for the upcoming fiscal year; and (b) the
draft investment budget for the education sector for the coming
three years. Both draft budgets shall be acceptable to IDA as
compared to the commitments made by the Government in the
Sectoral Adjustment Program (para. 3.3);
(b) open and maintain a Special Treasury Account for textbook renewal
on terms and conditions satisfactory to IDA; replenish this fund
by March 30 of each year, starting in 1990, with amounts
sufficient to cover the costs of developing and printing school
'extbooks during that year; and ensure that (a) the proceeds of
all textbook sales shall be deposited into this account, and (b)
all funds deposited into said account shall be used only to
defray the costs of developing and printing textbook titles for
primary and secondary schools (para. 3.10);
(c) by December 31, 1989, agree with IDA on the detailed program
chosen for the experiment with double-shift teaching; start the
experiment no later than October 31, 1990; and review with IDA
the final results of the experiment no later than September 30,
1991, and implement the recommendation of the said review in a
manner acceptable to IDA (para. 3.12);
(d) by June 30, 1989, agree with IDA on measures to ensure that
beneficiaries of boarding and restaurant facilities at the ISS
bear the costs of such services, and implement these measures
when said services have become operational (para. 3.19);
(e) by June 30, 1989, restrict the function of the ENS tot (i)
provision of pedagogical training; (ii) training in subject
matters pertaining to management of education resources and
facilities; and (iii) in-service training for education sector
staff (para. 3.20);
(f) by June 30, 1989: (i) establish the new CEP in Boghe and the new
commercial CEPILEP in Nouakchott with curricula and admission
criteria acceptable to IDA; and (ii) discuss and agree with IDA
on measures designed to involve employers in decisions on course
content and in financing parts of technical schools' operating
costs (para. 3.26);
(g) by June 30, 1989, review with IDA CFPM's draft curriculam and
measures for securing the financing of all CFPM's operating costs



- 28 -

(i) by September 30, 1990, develop an action program satisfactory to

(j) implement the project according to the arrangements and schedules
agreed upon during appraisal, including the procurement
procedures and the arrangements for project management,
accounting, auditing and reporting (paras. 3.36, 3.40, 3.42, and
3.46).
5.4       Conditions of credit effectiveness would be thats  (i) an
education planner has been employed in the DPC and that an architect and an
accountant have been employed in the PCU (para. 3.41); and (ii) conditions
for effectiveness of the cofinancing by the African Development Bank (AfDB)
have boen fulfilled.
5.5       Subject to the conditions set forth in paras. 5.1 to 5.4 above,
the project is suitable as a basis for an IDA credit of US$18.2 million
equivalent on standard terms.

ISLAMIC REPUBLIC OF MAURITANIA
EDUCATION SECTOR RESTRUCTURING PROJECT
Financial mDmlications of Educational Policies
1.        Background. To further the education dialogue between the
Government of Mauritania and the Bank Group, IDA organized in April 1987 a
three-day seminar in Nouakchott to review, together with Government
officials and staff of the various institutions of higher education,
alternative options for the development of education in Mauritania,
especially as relates to the tertiary level. As a background for these
discussions, simulations of the impact of alternative educational policies
were performed using the Bank's EDFISIMO Model. Further simulation
analyses were undertaken as background information for discussions of the
Government's education strategy held in June 1987, during the one-week
visit to Washington of a delegation of Mauritanian officials led by the
Minister of Education. These analyses and exchanges of views between IDA
and the Government served as important inputs into the formulation of the
education sector adjustment program that the Government has decided to
implement under the Education Sector Restructuring Project; they have also
served as basic elements of the Structural Adjustment Credit concerning the
Education Sector. The finalization of the simulation process was carried
out during the appraisal mission in January 1988, when the final numerical
valuea of the target policy indicators were determined on the basis of the
expenses effectively spent during Mauritania's 1987 fiscal year (ending
December 1987).
2.        AssumDtions behind the simulations.  The simulation model used
allows the analysis of the effects on primary school enrollments and
enrollment ratios of changes in the following parameterst
(a) Annual rate of growth in the Government's recurrent budget (GB),
(b) Share of GB devoted to education (EB),
(c) Annual growth rate of the first-cycle secondary education budget,
except for scholarships (BUD1),
(d) Annual growth rate of the second-cycle secondary education budget,
except for scholarships (BUD2),
(e) Annual growth rate of the technical secondary education budget (BUD3),
(f) Annual growth rate of the secondary education scholarship budget
(general and teacher training -- BUD4),
(g) Annual growth rate of the higher education budget. except for
scholarships for students in Mauritania and abroad (BUD5),



- 46 -
ANNEX 2-5
Page 2 of 4
(h) Annual growth rate of the higher education scholarship budget, for
students in Mauritania and abroad (BUD6),
(i) Annual growth rate of the central administrative budget (BUD7),
(j) Number of pupils per class in primary education, and
(k) Growth rate in the purchasing power of teachers' salaries (PS).
3.        Parameters (a) and (b) govern the share of the country's
resources devoted to public expenditures on education; parameters (c) to
(i) govern the allocation of education resources between levels, whereas
parameters (j) and (k) affect teacher salary costs per pupil in primary
education. Schematically, the model works in the following way. For given
initial values and future annual growth rates of GB, EB, and BUDI to BUD7
budgets, the model computes, as a residual, the amount of resources
available for primary education. Next, for given values of parameters (j)
and (k), the model calculates unit cost per pupil-year in primary
education, taking into account (1) costs of training material and of
administration and, most importantly, (2) the distribution of the teaching
force by qualification and salary scale level of each qualification
category. There exist currently three categories (moniteurs, instituteurs
adjoints, and instituteurs), but it is assumed that all newly recruited
teachers are instituteurs, given Mauritania's traiiing and recruitment
policy. Division of the total recurrent budget for primary education by
the unit cost thus derived, gives the total number of primary school
students that can be accommodated for a given budget and unit cost.  The
model also gives other outputs, such as new entrants into the primary
cycle, primary enrollment ratios, teacher requirements and construction
costs. It also takes into account enrollment in private education and
permits the simulation of the effects of changes in teaching loads and
curriculum hours on teacher requirements and, hence, on unit cost and
enrollment.
4.        Lessons.  To study the scope for expanding primary school
enrollment through a reallocation of resources in the education budget, a
number of simulations were carried out. The results of seven of these
co.-mputations are summarized in Table 1. The base year for the simulations
is 1987 (i.e., school year 1986/87 and fiscal year 1987), and all
assumptions regarding growth rates in budgets are in real terms.
5.        Simulation I shows that if the policies governing intrasectoral
resource allocations and unit costs were to remain unchanged, even under a
3Z annual growth rate in the government budget and education budget, the
primary enrollment ratio would decline from about 55Z in school year
1986/87, to 46Z in 1993/94 and 432 in the year 2000. Simulation IV --
which takes into account the cumulative effect of the policies described In
Simulations II, III and IV, i.e., (1) the freezing of higher and secondary
education scholarships (general and teacher training) at their 1987 levels,
(2) the freezing of higher education operating expenses at a level
corresponding to the 1987 budget, plus an 112 increase to take into account



- 47 -
ANNEX 2-5
Page 3 of 4
the new astatut des personnels" in higher education and a gradual
Mauritanization of teaching staff, and (3) containment of operating costs
in second-cycle secondary education resulting from the restrictions on new
admission to this level 1/ -- shows that the strategy decided upon by the
Mauritanian Government will allow primary enrollment to expand
substantially (from 150,443 in 1986/87 to 194,900 in 1993/94 and 266,800 in
the year 2000/01) and will prevent primary enrollment ratios from
decreasing in the medium term without increasing education's share of the
government budget.
6.        In order to increase primary enrollment ratios in the future,
additional actions need to be taken. The most effective measure would be
the reduction in unit costs. However, the scope for such a reduction is
limited, given the actual low quality of primary education and the fact
that the average class size is around 55. Simulations V and VI show the
impact of two measures through which increases in the primary enrollment
ratio can be obtained. Simulation V estimates the effect of an increase in
education's share of the government budget. It shows that a moderate
gradual increase in education's share of the government budget (from 22.42
in 1987 to 242 in 1993 and stabilization thereafter) can result in a
sizeable increase in primary enrollment ratios, if the extra resources are
allocated to primary schooling. Simulation VI shows that a decrease in
primary teacher salaries in real terms (22 per year for seven years and
stabilization later on) -- which means that salaries will increase over
this period but at a lower rate than inflation -- may have a substantial
quantitative impact on primary enrollment ratios, while maintaining
education's share of the government budget. It should be noted that a
decrease in teacher salaries is not among the measures included in the
Sectoral Adjustment Program agreed upon by the Government and the World
Bank (cf. Annex 3-1). However, it is a fact that during the last several
years the growth rate of teacher salaries has been below that of inflation.
7.        Finally, Simulation VII shows the impact of a 92 annual increase
in budgetary resources for primary education, which has been envisaged by
the Mauritanian Government.  The resulting impact on primary enrollment and
on primary enrollment ratios is substantial, creating enough places in
primary schools to accommodate the country's entire school age population
by the year 2000. The share of education in the government budget would,
however, have to increase from 22.42 in 1987 to 262 in 1993 and 292 in the
year 2000, coupled with a policy of constantly allocating almost all new
resources to primary education. Naturally, in the long run, a policy that
exclusively favors primary education is not realistic.
b:ann2-5.txt (min)
1/   Confer Annex 3-1 which describes the different policy measures
included in the Sectoral Adjustment Program agreed upon between the
Government of Mauritania and the World Bank.

REPUBLIQUE ISLAMIQUE DE MAURITANIE
PROJET DE RESTRUCTURATION DU SECTEUR DE LEDUCATION
Programme de Construction et d'Entretien des Ecoles Primaires
A. Diagnostic du contexte actuel
1.        Situation du reseau scolaire.  La Mauritanie, dot4e d'un des
taux de scolarisation primaire lee plus faibles d'Afrique (532), vient
d'enregistrer au cours des dix dernieres annees un accroissement accEldrd

mformules locales' en ce qui concerne les mat&riaux et les modiles

B. Stratdaie propos4e pour le projet
4.        Tenant compte du diagnostic precedent et de l'un des objectifs
principaux du projet qui consiste a creer les conditions propices a une
augmentation durable du taux de scolarisation et & ameliorer la qualite et
la pertinence de 1Venseignement fondamental, le projet se propose
d'intervenir sur deux plans: (a) le developpement de la capacite
d'accueil de l'enseigneiaent fondamental elargie et rationalisee; et (b) le
renforcement des structures administratives regionales.
5.        Afin d'augmenter la capacite d'accueil des infrastructures
scolaires, le projet prevoit la strategie suivante:
-    la realisation en plusieurs phases d'etudes de carte scolaire qui
doivent cerner la taille et la localisation des interventions de
construction/rehabilitation ou suppression de classes & effectifs
trop reduits;
-    la suppression des classes dont lea effec* ifs sont inferieurs au
seuil de 35 eleves;
-    l'introduction d'un systeme de double vacation des locaux ayant des
effectifs superieurs a 70 eleves par classe;
-    le recrutement tous les deux ou trois ans au lieu de tous les six ans
dans lea ecoles a classe unique;
-    la sauvegarde des investissements dej3  rdalises, sauf pour les ecoles
de faible frequentation par la mise en place de programmes de
rehabilitation et d'entretien;
-    la stimulation de l'apport des collectivites en accompagnant leurs
actions engagees en matiere de constructions scolaires par des
actions complementaires; toutefois le projet n'interviendra seulement
lorsqu'un engagement de contrepartie de la part des communes sera
assure; et
-    la reduction des besoins de remplacement et des coots d'amortissement
des installations scolaires, en developpant des techniques de
construction et d'entretien appropriees.
6.        Afin de renforcer le cadre institutionnel dont depend la mise en
oeuvre de cette st-ategie, le projet prevoit le renforcement des
structures administratives suivantes:
-    au niveau central par la restructuration du Service de Planification
et des Constructions (SPC) charge egalement des travaux de carte

C.   Justification economigue
10.       Selon la strategie developpee ci-dessug, le coUt pour l'Etat

11        Le coat annuel d'amortissement par el&ve est neanmoins

Economiquement justifieo, en particulier sail n'existe que 20 a 35 eleves
a scolariser localement (compte tenu de la faible densite de population).
12.       Par contre dans les situations oil il existe un nombre plus
important d'enfants a scolAriser et oft lea communautes sont disposees a

justification d'une salle de classe, on observe que la construction d'une,

l'on soe trouve dans le cas d'effectifs suffisants (au-del& de 35 eleves),

17.       Svsthme d'entretien des ecoles.  Afin de reduire les besoins de
remplacement des installations scolaires du primaire et de reduire les
cotts de leur remise en 6tat, le projet prevoit le transfert de
responsabilite de leur entretien courant aux etablissements. Dans cette
perspective, le projet prevoit de fournir a chacun des 898 etablissements
situ6s dans les regions concernees par le projet les appuis techniques
suivants: (i) un guide d'entretien decrivant les taches regulieres a
accomplir et la maniere de r4pondre a des degats mineurs impr4visibles;
(ii) un livret d'entretien pour le suivi des performances des responsables
d'etablissement; (iii) une trousse a outils; et (iv) des conseils
*pisodiques, notamment a l'occasion des interventions du programme de
construction.
18.       Afin de soutenir l'exdcution du programme de construction et de
promouvoir les options adoptdes par le Gouvernement en la matiere, le
projet prevoit la restructuration du SPC et sa decentralisation au niveau
rjgional.
19.       Service de Planification et des Constructions Scolaires (SPC).
Le credit financera les besoins additionnels du Service en mati6re
d'4quipement, de vehicules, d'ass'stance technique (trois hommes-annees),
de services de specialistes pour des appuis ponctuels (trois hommes-mois),
de formation et de coftts de fonctionnement.
20.       Cellules mobiles.  Deux cellules mobiles interviendront en appui
aus DREFs; elles seront chargees exclusivement de faciliter et organiser
sur le terrain l'ex4cution du programme de construction de salles de
classe; elle seront basdes l'une a Nouakchott pour l'axe nord-sud et
l'autre & Kiffa pour l'axe est-ouest et seront dotees d'equipements et
vehicules necessaires, de materiel de chantier et d'un budget de
fonctionnement annuel pour la duree du projet. Le credit financera
dgalem_nt la remuneration de trois techniciens superieurs (contractuels
nationaux) durant cinq ans et de deux volontaires durant quatre ans ainsi
que les services de spdcialistes pour des appuis ponctuels (15
homues-mois) et les supports pedagogiques aux actions d'information, de
sensibilisation et de formation (voir le tableau no. 8 donnant le
calendrier de mise en place des techniciens).

ISLAMIC REPUBLIC OF MAURITANIA
EDUCATION SECTOR RESTRUCTURING PROJECT
Textbook Development Proaram for Primary and
General Secondary Education
1.        Background.  Until now, the Government has aimed at supplying,
free of charge, textbooks and teachers' guides for all subjects taught in
primary and general secondary education. However, although a number of
textbook manuscripts are being developed by the National Pedagogical
Institute (IPN), a lack of resources has resulted in that very few of these
manuscripts have actually reached the stage of publication and distribution
to schools. As a consequence, there is a severe shortage of textbooks in
schools. When available, the ratio of students per textbook at the primary
level for Arab language classes (81Z of total primary enrollment) is three,
with an even higher scarcity in rural areas. There are practically no
books available to bilingual section students. Furthermore, for many
subjects there are no books at all. The provision of teachers, guides is
inadequate, and those available are often outdated.
2.        At the secondary level, the scarcity of textbooks is particularly
acute in science subjects in the second cycle of general secondary
education. This cycle is divided into three streams: Stream A is a
literary branch, subdivided into literature (AA) and modern literature
(AB); Stream C is a scientific branch, stressing mathematics, physics and
chemistry; and Stream D is a natural science branch, including a strong
basis in mathematics, physics and chemistry. In 1986-87, the distribution
of students among these three streams was, respectively, 45Z, 14Z and 412.
Of the total enrollment of 13,812 second cycle students, 692 followed Arab
language sections and 312 the French ("bilingualn) sections.  Science
textbooks are practically non-existent for the former section. Some French
books are available (mainly through gifts from France), however they are
unevenly distributed and do not correspond to existing Mauritanian
programs. Existing Arabic language textbooks in science subjects do not
correspond to Mauritanian needs, especially since the Maghreb countries
have not yet gone as far as Mauritania in Arabization of their science
teaching. In addition, Mauritania is currently revising its primary and
secondary education programs, requiring corresponding changes in textbooks
and teachers' guides.
3.        IPN is responsible for the development and procurement of
pedagogical materials and for the provision of in-service training for
teachers. The Institute has a total staff of 97, of whom 42 (32
pedagogical advisors and 10 expatriate specialists) carry out _.&e writing,
editing and publication of textbooks. Local production of textbooks is a
lengthy three-year processt one year for development, one year for



- 71 -
ANNEX 3-3
Page 2 of 9
testing. and one year for editing, production and distribution. IPN's work
program includes the development of as many as 87 textbook titles for the
primary, secondary and technical levels.  Many are at an advanced stage of
development, some are awaiting testing, and some are ready for printing;
however, IPN's lack of financial resources is delaying the completion of
the process.
4.        To produce the original of a textbook and the copies for
distribution to teachers for testing purposes, IPN has a small workshop
equipped with an offset printing machine. Once tested and reviewed, the
textbooks developed by IPM are edited and printed abroad. A printshop,
financed by Saudi Arabia, is now under construction adjacent to IPN and is
expected to be operational by 1990.  It is then expected that material
produced by IPN in short series will be printed at this new printshop,
while large-scale printing will continue to be done abroad.
5.        Obiectives.  The overall objective of this project element is to
improve the efficiency of the learning process in primary education, and of
science teaching in the second cycle of secondary education, through
strengthening the national capacity to develop, procure and distribute
low-cost textiooks and teachers' guides adapted to the Mauritanian
socio-cultural enviroranent. In addition, for primary education, the
project provides a tminimum set of basic pedagogical equipment to all
primary classrooms. More specifically, the project aims to:
for primary education:
(a) complete the development and testing of 24 textbook titles in
mathematics and reading for primary education (one book in Arabic and
one in French, per grade and per subject);
(b) print about 350,000 books from these 24 titles;
(c)  develop and print about 60,000 teachers' guides in reading, grammar,
mathematics, local studies, oral expression and physical education;
(d) provision of a minimum set of basic equipment to all existing (2,740)
primary education classrooms (the composition of this equipment is
described in Annex 3-2);
for second cycle secondary education:
(e) develop and test 12 textbook titles in Arabic (c.ne book for each of
the three grades in, respectively, physics, chemistry, natural
sciences and mathematics);
(f) print about 120,000 books from these 12 titles (about 10,000 per
title);



- 72 -
ANNEX 3-3
Page 3 of 9
for both levels of education:
(g) strengthen the existing storage and distributicn system at IPN and at
the regional inspectorates (DREFs).
6.        Required Outcome.  The outcome of this project element is to
ensure that a nucleus of competence is created in educational publishing
and, thereby, to improve the quality of instruction and the efficiency of
resource utilization. By the end of the project, it is expected that one
textbook will be available for two pupils in mathematics and reading in
primary education, and that all pupils in the second cycle of general
secondary education will have textbooks in physics, chemistry, natural
sciences and mathematics.
7.        Strategy.  The textbook component of the project covers three
main groups of activities:
(a) Textbook development, experimentation and publishing: development
over the medium term of a managerial and technical capability within
INP to write and test: (i) textbooks in reading and mathematics for
all grades of primary education, and in physics, chemistry, natural
sciences and mathematics for the second cycle of general secondary
education; and (ii) teachers' guides for primary education in reading,
grammar, mathemiatics, local studies, oral expression and physical
education;
(b) Textbook procurement and distribution: strengthening the capability
to procure and distribute textbooks and teachers' guides; and
(c) Textbook storage: improvement of storage facilities for textbooks at
INP and regional inspectorates.
8.        The development of the national capacity to develop, test and
publish textbooks and teachers' guides would include thes
(a) establishment of a production unit within INP to prepare for local
publishing and procurement of books;
(b) adoption of publication targets and an overall program of book
production;
(c) renovation of equipment at IPN and provision of operating costs and
two vehicles to improve textbook distribution;
(d) provision of storage facilities; and
(e) establishment of a revolving fund out of proceeds from the sales of
books to pupils.



- 73 -
ANNEX 3-3
Page 4 of 9
Full details of the above are available in the Project Preparation
Document. In addition, Table 1 of this Annex provides a summary list of
equipment and furniture to be provided under the project; Table 2 shows the
production schedule for textbooks and teachers' guides for primary
education; Table 3 gives the production schedule for textbooks for general
secondary education; Table 4 shows the timetable for strengthening IPN;
while Table 5 summarizes quantities and costs.
9.        Coordination and Management.  The implementation and daily
management of this project element will be undertaken by IPN since it is
already responsible for the production, procurement and distribution of
textbooks and teachers' guides. However, textbook approval is the
responsiblity of a National Textbook Committee chaired by the Director of
IPN and whose members represent all parties concerned, i.e., the Ministry
of Education, DREF and IPN.

ISLAMIC REPUBLIC OF MAURITANIA
EDUCATION SECTOR RESTRUCTURING PROJECT
In-service Teaching Training in Primary and
General Secondary Education
1.        Background.  Although as much as 812 of Mauritania's 3,010
primary school teachers are instituteurs (i.e., have completed lower
secondary education plus three years of teacher training), there is a need
to update the knowledge of teachers in areas such as teaching methods and
revision of curriculum. Furthermore, it is urgent to train teachers in
areas not covered by existing teacher training programs, e.g., use of new
textbooks and teachers' guides, special skills required for use of
double-shift and multi-grade teaching, and the role of teachers in
preventive maintenance of school buildings.
2.        In school year 1986/87, there were 711 teachers in the second
cycle of general secondary education, of whom 510 were Mauritanians; of
these 510 teachers, about 220 were teaching science subjects. About
two-thirds of the Mauritanian science teachers are trained through a
four-year program at the Ecole Normale Superieure (ENS) following the
baccalaureat, while the remaining one-third are first-cycle (CEG) teachers
who, after at least three years of teaching experience, were admitted to a
two-year ENS program. 1/ The scientific background of existing science
teachers is highly theoretical, and few have had the occasion to conduct
the type of science experiments they are supposed to teach to their pupils
according to the existing curriculum. Therefore, it is imperative to train
these teachers in the use of science equipment to be provided under the
project prior to procurement of this equipment.
3.        Objectives.  In support of the overall project objective of
improving the quality of primary education and of science teaching in the
second cycle of general secondary education, this project element aims at
(i) upgrading the skills of primary school teachers, and at (ii) enabling
science teachers in the lycees to efficiently use the science equipment
provided under the project.
4.        Required outcomes.  Coupled with the provision of textbooks and
teachers' guides in primary education, and with the provision of textbooks
and science equipment in general secondary education, this project
1/   In the future, science teachers will receive their subject matter
training at the ISS, followed by one year of pedagogical training at
the ENS.



- 80 -
ANNEX 3-4
Page 2 of 3
component is expected to have a synergetic impact on the effectiveness of
the teaching process at these two levels of education.
5.        Strategy.  As regards primary education, the project provides for
two weeks of retraining for about 3,000 teachers (i.e., the total 1986/87
teaching force). The two-week duration is an estimated average for all
retraining activities to be undertaken for p imary school teachers. It is
understood that the training should be tailor-made according to the needs
of different categories of teachers, and that some categories may need
longer periods of training than other cateogories. The retraining
activities would be organized during summer vacations, either at the two
primary teacher training colleges (ENIs) or at the DREFs, and would be
phased throughout the duration of the project. At the outset, priority
would be given to the least qualified teachers, i.e., the moniteurs (196 in
1986/87) and the instituteurs-adjoints (387 in 1986/87).
6.        The courses would update the knowledge of teachers in areas such
as pedagogical methods and revision of primary education curriculum. The
courses would also cover topics such as the use of new textbooks and
teachers' guides, use of multi-grade and double-shift teaching methods, and
the teachers' role in preventive maintenance of school buildings. A
national Task Force would be established to determine training content and
the modalities of organizing the courses. It is expected that the trainers
will be drawn from the staff of the two ENIs.
7.        As regards general secondary education, the project provides for
about 24 days of retraining for about 220 teachers (120 in physics and
chemistry and 100 in natural sciences). The courses would be held during
the summer holidays in 1989 and 1990. Their main objectives are to
familiarize science teachers with the revised curriculum and, most
importantly, to train them in how to use the scientific equipment provided
by the project to conduct practical experiments contained in this
curriculum. The courses would be organized at the ENS, using this school's
facilities and staff.
8.        Coordination and management.  The IPN bears the main
ret. onsibility for in-service teacher training in Mauritania. For the
retraining of primary school teachers, the courses provided for under the
project would be organized by the IPN in cooperation with the Directorate
of Primary Education and the ENIs. For science teachers in secondary
education, the courses would be organized by the IPN in cooperation with
the Directorate of Secondary Education and the EN.
9.        Costs.  The approximate costs for organizing thn training of the
3,000 primary school teachers have been derived as follows:

ISLAMIC REP'UBLIC OF MAURITANIA
EDUCATION SECTOR RESTRUCTURING PROJECT
Experimentation with Double-Shift Teaching in Primary Schools
1.        Background.  In Mauritania, primary school class size differs
markedly between urban and rural areas. In the former, classes are generally
overcrowded as a consequence of the steady increase in urban population (due
to migrations from rural areas following the recent drought and subsequent
rapid sedentarization of the nomadic populations) and strong private demand
for education. In many rural areas, classes are comparatively small,
especially in low population density zones where nomadic populations are still
predominant.
2.        At the regional level, the average number of pupils per class for
all grades in primary education varies from 38 in Inchiri to 81 in Nouakchott.
At intraregional levels, variations among different locations are even wider.
Furthermore, substantial differences also exist among different grades, the
average class size being larger the lower the grade. For example in Inchiri,
the average class size is 62 for Grades 1 and 2, and 33 for Grades 5 and 6.
When analyzing the overall distribution of primary class s_.ze at the country
level, it appears that 27Z of classes has less than 35 pupils, while 42? of
the school population is accommodated in classes of over 70 pupils.
3.        In an effort to promote efficiency in the use of public resources
for education, the Government will undertake a school mapping exercise in
order to better document the regional distribution of the enrolled and
non-enrolled population (see Annex 3-12). The results from this exercise will
be used as a basis for extending the use of multi-grade teaching in areas
where class size is low, and of double-shift teaching in urban areas.
4.        Obiective of the Experiment.  The overall objective of this project
element is to assist the Government first, to assess the feasibility for
double-shift teaching and second, to develop and adopt a policy to implement
it.
5.        Strategy.  The strategy t3 be adopted follows a four-point program:
(a) initial assessment of alternative options for double-shift teaching and
selection of the options to be tested;
(b) implementation of the experiment in about 30 classes in Nouakchott;
(c) careful evaluation of the outcome of the experiment in terms of its
impact on student achievement, cost savings and institutional and
logistical problems; and
(d) policy-oriented assessment of the results of (c), and adoption of a
government policy for implementing double-shift teaching in relevant
areas throughout the country.



- 83 -
ANNEX 3-5
Page 2 of 3
6.        To implement this program, a Task Force of four or five nationals
drawn from the Ministry of Education (Direction de l'Enseignement Fondamental,
Direction de la Planification, Institut Pedagogique National, Corps
d'Inspection de l'Enseignement Fondamental) will be established. This Task
Force will take the lead in the experiment through a part-time but continuous
commitment throughout its preparation, implementation, evaluation and
assessment of its potential for extension to other areas. This group will be
assisted at the various stages by two consultants -- an expert in curriculum
design and school timetabling, and an expert in education evaluation
(educational researcher).
7.        As regards the initial assessment of alternative options, the
project would -- following a careful review of alternative, feasible options
for double-shift teaching in urban primary school environments -- select one
or more approaches, including one based on a reduction in the number of daily
hours of instruction (possibly increasing the number of days in the school
year) and corresponding changes in curricula (concentration in basic subjects,
such as reading, writing and arithmetic), so as to allow a teacher (receiving
financial compensation for his extra duties) to teach two different groups of
pupils per day. Preliminary analyses suggest that the most cost-efficient
double-shift system implies a reduction in the number of daily teaching hours
provided to pupils, and that this is most acceptable in the three lower
grades. By way of illustration, one possible option would be to reduce the
weekly hours of instruction from the present 30 hours to, for instance, 18 in
Grades 1, 2 and 3, and to increase the number of hours taught by teachers from
30 to 36 per week (compensating the teachers accordingly), so that each
teacher would teach two groups of pupils per day. A major objective of the
experiment would be to evaluate whether possible negutive effects of the
reduction in the number of class hours can be compensated for by factors such
as smaller class size and more and better training material.
8.        Once the options have been selected, the experiment will have to be
properly designed (i.e., which schools in Nouakchott will be involved, which
changes in curricula will be implemented, and how will the teachers' time be
organized) and implemented (training of teachers prior to carrying out the
experiment, distribution of the relevant, specific teaching materials, etc.).
9.        To ensure that parents, primary level pupils, schools and staff
understand the reasons behind and objectives of the experimentation with
double-shift teaching, a short campaign will be mounted to sensitize public
opinion to the importance of the experiment.
10.       The experiment should begin in test classes (about 30) no later than
school year 1990/91. Student evaluation instruments will have to be ready by
this date. A control group of about 20 classes will also take part in the
evaluation as a means to test the impact of double-shift teaching by reference
to the standard teaching method. The operation of schools in the experimental
group will be assessed during the school year by way of questionnaires and
obserr7ations. The individual achievement of all participating students will
be measured by means of tests reflecting the curriculum content for both the
experimental and the control group. The results will be analyzed in such a
way that it will be possible to sort out (and to list the significance of) the
impact of double-shift teaching on student achievement, taking into account
other sources of influence (teacher and student characteristics, school



- 84 -
ANNEX 3-5
Page 3 of 3
inputs, etc.). A careful report on the results will be produced, made
available and discussed in a seminar led by the Task Force.
11.       Finally, the Task Force, possibly with the participation of
policy-oriented officials, will analyze the results of the experiment and the
opinions of seminar participants, and will assess both costs and benefits (in
terms of student achievement, unit costs, institutional problems) of
double-shift teaching with the possibility of extending this method to other
areas (how many schools could be involved and in which locations). Proposals
will, thereafter, be made for a country-wide policy.
12.       Timetable.  By end December 1989, the Task Force will prepare a
report examining different feasible options for double-shift teaching and
recommending one approach for experimentation. The experiment should begin no
later than October 31, 1990. It should be conducted during one school year,
probably in the first three grades in about 30 classrooms; and the evaluation
of the results and their policy implications should be reviewed with IDA no
later than September 30, 1991, with a view to developing a system that could
be introduced as from the start of school year 1992/93.
13.       Administration and management.  The experimentation with
double-shift teaching will be administered by the Task Force, assisted by two
short-term consultants. The Task Force will liaise with relevant persons and
institutions in Mauritania to ensure a comprehensive and coherent approach.
14.       Means.  The table below shows the base costs for the experiment to
be financed under the project.

ISLAMIC REPUBLIC OF MAURITANIA
EDUCATION SECTOR RESTRUCTURING PROJECT
Strengthening of Regional Inspectorates (DREF)
1.        Background.  At the regional level, the management of primary
education is the responsibility of the Directions Regionales de
l'Enseignement Fondamental (DREF), which are located in the regional
capitals (12 regions plus the District of Nouakchott) and report to the
Director of Primary Education in the Ministry of Education. Each DREF
contains four administrative sections dealing with:
-    Curriculum and Pedagogical Matters;
-    Personnel Management;
-   Management of Pedagogical Materials and Equipment; and
-    Statistics and Planning.
2.        The number of staff in each DREF depends on the importance of the
region in terms of school enrollment; however, the average staffing is
about 8-12 persons, including a director, one or more inspectors, one
person responsible for equipment, two or more persons (normally trained as
teachers) dealing with statistics and planning tasks, and support staff
(2-3 secretaries/typists, a driver and 2-4 orderlies (day laborers)). Some
DREFs also have a carpenter.
3.        As suggested by the above administrative structure and staffing,
the main responsibilities of the DREFs relate to:
-    administrative and pedagogical support and control of primary
education;
-    experimentation of textbooks developed by the National
Pedagogical Institute (IPN);
-    personnel administration as regards primary education;
-    collection of education statistics; and
-    technical support to communities as regards school construction
and school furniture. This role is presently performed in a very
limited way.
In short, whereas the General Inspection defines curricula content and
timetables, and the IPN develops training materials and organizes
in-service teacher training, the DREFs are responsible for the
implementation and monitoring of the training provided.
4.        In view of the very large geographical distances in Mauritania,
the DREFs have a major role to play in the implementation of this project
as regards the expansion and quality improvement of primary education.
However, while most DREFs are adequately staffed, they lack the physical
means required to carry out their work.



- 86 -
AMNM 3-6
Paso 2 of 2
5.           Obiectives.  The objective  of this project elemnt is to
strengthen the capacity of the DRZPs tot (a) control resource use at the
regional and school level; (b) provide pedagogical support to teachers and
monitor the quality of instruction; (c) together with IPM, organize
in-service training for teachers and school directors; and (d) together
with the School Construction Service of the DPC (see Annex 3-2), supervise
school construction, rehabilitation and maintenance.
6.           Strategy.   To achieve the above objectives,  the project would:
(a) renovate the buildings of three of the DREPs and construct new
buildings for the remaining ten; (b) provide all 13 DREFs with basic
equipment and one vehicle each; tc) provide the  ElN's school furniture
manufacturing workshop in Rosso with materials and supplies; (d) provide
in-service training for DREP staff -- included under the support for IPN
(see Annex 3-3) and for the DPC (see Annex 3-12); and (e) cover incremental
recurrent costs related to the operation and maintenance of the vehicles.
7.           Costs.  Table 1 summarizes the project's  support for the DREFs
(apart from training of staff as indicated above). Annex 3 18 provides
further details on the civil works element.

ISLAMIC REPUBLIC OF MAURITANIA
EDUCATION SECTOR RESTRUCTURING PROJECT
Science Teaching in General Secondary Education
1.        Background.  As of school year 1986/87, Mauritania had 23
colleges and 20 lycees providing general secondary education. Colleges
provide first cycle education (i.e., the first three grades of general
secondary education), and lycees provide second cycle education, (i.e., the
last three grades). The proposed project is geared mainly toward the
improvement of second cycle science education, although the provision of
textbooks and basic kits of science equipment, as well as the renovation of
classrooms, will also benefit first cycle science education.
2.        The second cycle of general secondary education is divided into
three main streams of study: Stream A is a literary branch; Strean, C is a
scientific branch which stresses mathematics, physics and chemistry; and
Stream D is a natural science branch, which includes a strong basis in
mathematics. physics and chemistry. In 1986/87, the second cycle had
13,812 students of whom 45Z were enrolled in Stream A, 14? in Stream C, and
41? in Stream D (see Table 1 of Annex 2-4). The pupil/teacher ratio at the
secondary level was, on average, 24 students per teacher, although in some
lycees this ratio averaged 50 students per teacher.
3.        In general, secondary science education programs in Mauritania
are outdated -- they are based on those used in France some 20 years ago --
and they do not correspond well to present and future development needs of
the country. The teaching of science is mainly theoretical, reflecting a
similar bias in teacher training and lack of equipment and supplies needed
for pupils to perform practical experiments. Laboratory sessions are
generally not provided because of a lack of supplies and equipment, and
practically all of the about 20 laboratories that exist in 12 lycees are in
such a deteriorated state that they cannot be used at all.   As a result,
most students never have the occasion to perform practical experiments
during the course of their studies. Furthermore, field trips, which are
also an essential element of natural science teaching, are not carried out
due to the lack of transportation.
4.        Textbooks are also in very short supply, and most school
libraries are insufficiently stocked with books. For instance, only 16 of
the existing 42 lyc6es and colleges have a school library, and some of
these libraires have only an average of 50 books for 400 students. The
scarcity of textbooks forces teachers to spend most of their teaching time
dictating courses, instead of adequately preparing students for entry into
higher-level education at home or abroad.
5.        A result of worsening teaching conditions in the Mauritanian
lyc4es has been a sharp decline in the success rate of the baccalaureat,



- 88 -
ANNEX 3-7
Page 2 of 6
from 50.21 in 1980 to 25.85 in 1987. The declinP 'ms been particularly
marked for the natural science stream, from 50.02 in 1980 to 21.52 in 1987.
6.        Obiectives.  The overall objective of this project component is
to improte the quality of science teaching in general secondary education,
especial   in the second cycle, by providing in-service training for
teachers, supplying pedagogical equipment and materials, renovating 100
existing classrooms, and establishing three pilot laboratory blocks in
secondary schools in order to help define the conditions and modalities
under which the Government could provide, in the future, some kind of
minimum laboratory facilities to all schools.
7.        Required Outcome.  The outcome of this project component will be
the improvement of the quality of science teaching in general secondary
education and the provision of science programs that better prepare
baccalaureat graduates for higher education, as well as for the labor
market.
8.        Strategy.  To assist the Ministrv of Education in strengthening
science teaching in secondary schools, the proi3ct would: (a) revise and
update the science curriculum in the second cycle of secondary education;
(b) provide for the development, publication and distribution of 12
textbook titles in Arabic -- one book for each of the three grades in,
respectively, physics, chemistry, natural sciences and mathematics;
(c, establish three pilot science laboratory blocks to define the
conditions and modalities under which the Government could generalize the
provision of laboratories to all lycees. A science laboratory block would
include two laboratories -- one used mainly for physics, the other for
chemistry and natural sciences -- with a storeroom in between. A fourth
science block is currently being established in Naouakchott through French
bilateral assistance; (d) equip these three laboratory blocks and, in
addition, provide a minimum kit of physics, chemistry and natural science
equipment to all secondary schools (colleges and lycees) that do not have
laboratories to be used in regular classrooms. This basic kit is intended
to be used for teacher demonstrations of important experiments; (e) provide
in-service training for all existing science teachers (about 220) to
familiarize them with the revised curricula, the new textbooks and the
laboratory equipment; and (f) provide basic supplies (chemicals, test
tubes, etc.) for the duration of the project for the three pilot
laboratories and for the schools receiPring the basic kit.
9.        Table 1 shows the timetable for implementing the elements of this
strategy referring to points (c), (d) and (f) above. The textbook element
is described in Annex 3-3, while the element on in-service training is
dealt with in Annex 3-4. As indicated in Table 1, the revision of
programs, which has already started, is expected to be completed during
1989. During 1988, the set of basic experiments to be conducted in
physics, chemistry and natural sciences should be selected and developed by
a national commission appointed specifically for this purpose. Following
the decision on which experiments are to be conducted by pupils in each of
the three grades of the lycee, the lists of basic equipment for the three



- 89 -
ANNEX 3-7
Page 3 of 6
pilot science blocks and for the basic kits should be finalized. Decisions
also need to be made on factors such as whether it is less costly to use
bottled gas in the science blocks or have fixed installations; and, for
supplies, whether to buy chemicals of "technical quality* or "pure
quality,. the former being much cheaper than the latter although both are
of similar quality.
10.       The construction of the scienice blocks should be completed before
the arrival of the equipment. In order to ensure proper use and
maintenance of this equipment as well r.s of that contained in the basic
kits, the following measures will be taken. First, one laboratory
technician will be recruited for each of the .aree science blocks to
maintain and supervise use of the equipment, and to make small repairs.
Such technicans are currently being trained through a two-year course at
the ISS to be completed in January 1989. Second, one teacher per school
will be designated as responsible for the equipment provided through the
science blocks and the kits. Third, the Government will employ one or two
inspectors who will travel throughout the country to ensure that the
equipment provided is propserly used and maintained, and that the basic
supplies needed to use this equipment are replenished in a timely manner.
Fourth, well in advance of the arrival of the equipment, the Ministry of
Education should take the necessary measures to protect it from theft, as
well as sand and dust, through the preparation of a storeroom equipped for
this purpose.
11.       Coordination and management.  The Directorate of Secondary
Education in the Ministry of Education will bear the main responsibility
for this component as far as the pilot science blocks and equipment are
concerned. The National Pedagogical Institute will be responsible for the
textbook and in-service training elements, while the Inspector General will
be responsible for the revision of programs.
12.       Evaluation.  The Directorate of Secondary Education will
establish a small group of people involved in the teaching of science to
evaluate, on an ongoing basis, the experiment with the three pilot science
blocks and the basic kits.
13.       Costs and quantities.  The detailed costs and quantities for
textbooks and in-service training are given in Annexes 3-3 and 3-4; those
referring to the construction of the three science blocks and the
renovation of 100 classrooms are given in Annex 3-18. The basis for
estimating the costs for the science blocks and basic kits are given below;
Table 2 summarizes total costs of the component.

ISLAWIC REPUBLIC OF MAURITANIA
EDUCATION SECTOR RESTRUCTURING PROJECT
Science Teaching in Higher Education
1.        Background.  Teaching of science and technology at the tertiary
level is provided by three institutions which are all under the Ministry of
Education: (a) The Higher Institute of Science (Institut Superieur des
Sciences -- ISS); (b) The Secondary Teacher Training College (Ecole
Normale Superieure -- ENS); and (c) The Higher Center for Technical
Education (Centre Superieur d'Enseignement Technique -- CSET). All three
institutions are to be strengthened under the project. This Annex explains
the support to be given to the ISS and the ENS, while support for the CSET
is dealt with in Annex 3-9.
ISS
2.        In November 1986, the Government introduced reforms to:
(a) eliminate excess training capacity for secondary school teachers by
merging the programs provided at the Lower Secondary Teacher Trai-'-ing
College (CFP/CEG) with those provided at the Higher Secondary Teacher
Training College (ENS); and (b) strengthen the teaching of applied science
in higher education by creating a new Higher Institute of Sciences (ISS).
The new ENS, resulting from the merger of the two former teacher training
institutions, took over the facilities of the CFPICEG, while the ISS was
located in the facilities of the former ENS. Under the present project,
the ISS will be transferred to Rosso to occupy tha buildings of the Rosso
Lycde, which will be renovated and expanded to accommodate the ISS. As
explained elsewhere, the Lycee would be transferred to the ENI buildings in
Rosso which, in turn, would be transferred to Aloun where new facilities
would be constructed.
3.        Programs offered at ISS are divided into three main categories:
(a) environment and food security; (b) exploitation of natural resources;
and (c) communication and information sciences. The training would be
provided through two two-year cycles. Students would receive general
training in science and technology during the first cycle, and would
specialize in areas such as mining, agronomy and veterinary sciences,
renewable energy, hydrology and environmental sciences during the second
cycle. Students may interrupt their studies to pursue practical,
on-the-job training or to prepare for entrance exams to other institutes
(e.g., the ENS). A third cycle would be established for students wishing
to pursue post-graduate degrees (DEA), and eventually a doctorate program
in conjunction with a foreign university. The ISS programs are being
developed by its staff in collaboration with the University of Nice.
Further details on these programs are given in Annex IV-6 of the Project
Preparation Document.



- 94 -
ANNEX 3-8
Page 2 of 4
4.        The total number of students at the ISS should not exceed 600
students per year during the implementation of the project.
5.        Pedagogical equipment and materials available in present ISS
laboratories for biology, geology, physics and chemistry are generally well
maintained, but not sufficient; there is practically no research equipment,
nor is there equipment available for physiology, microbiology and
information sciences. Audiovisual equipment is old and needs to be
replaced, and there is a severe shortage of textbooks and reference
materials in the ISS library.
ENS
6.        The former ENS was created in 1970 to train teachers for the
second cycle of general secondary education through four-year
post-secondary programs. Following the merger of the CFP/CEG in November
1986, and the creation of the ISS, the new ENS will provide one-year
pedagogical training for ISS and University graduates who, after completion
of this one-year program, will teach in first or second cycle general
secondary education. The new ENS is located in the buildings of the former
CFP/CEG constructed in 1982-83 under the Second Education Project. This
recent infrastructure is well equipped and only a limited amount of
supplementary pedagogical materials and equipment will need to be provided
by the present project.
7.        In 1986/87, ENS had a total of 298 students of which 123 were
enrolled in science programs. This was, however, a year of transition and,
given the shortening of programs from two to one year and the excess supply
of secondary school teachers in some fields, the Government will conduct a
detailed study to assess future training needs, including those arising
from replacing most of the foreign secondary school teachers with nationals
and from retraining existing teachers.
8.        Obiectives.  The overall objective of this project component is
to (a) help Mauritania create a capacity to conduct university-level
training and research in the field of natural sciences through the
establishment of the ISS in Rosso in facilities equipped for this type of
training and research; and (b) strengthen the training of secondary school
science teachers through provision of supplementary pedagogical equipment
and materials for the ENS.
9.        Required Outcome.  By orienting a part of higher education
students away from humanities and social science studies towards natural
science programs, and by improving the quality of se-ndary school science
teachers, this project component is expected to im;   ' the quality and
relevance of secondary and higher education in Haur       a.
10.       Strateav.  The project would enable the Go   iment to transfer
the ISS to Rosso and to install the Institute in buila .gs properly
equipped for science teaching and research. To this end, the project
would: (a) renovate the following existing facilities in Rosso: about 620



- 95 -
ANNEX 3-8
Page 3 of 4
m2 of administrative space, 2,620 m2 of teaching space. boarding facilities
for 120 students, 16 lodgings for permanent teachers and 10 for visitors,
and 390 m2 of lodging for technical employees (cf. Annex 3-18 for further
details); (b) new construction of about 930 m2 of teaching space and of a
university restaurant with about 200 student places (780 m2); (c) purchase
of one vehicle; (d) equipment for eight laboratories (mainly research
equipment and to supplement equipment transferred from existing
laboratories in Nouakchott) in the following fields: animal biology and
microbiology, geology, pedology, plant biology, computer technology,
chemistry and energetics, biophysics and energetics, and soil technology.
The project would also equip an experimental garden and an "animalerie";
(e) provision for replenishment and maintenance of laboratory supplies and
equipment; (f) printing of two reference books, one on the flora of
Mauritania, the other on geology, and purchase of about 3,000 textbooks;
(g) provision for training abroad of 12 teachers/technicians through
two-month courses; and (h) provision of six man-months of technical
assistance to install laboratory equipment and demonstrate how to operate
it.
11.       As regards the ENS, the project would provide:  (a) supplementary
enuipment for physics and chemistry laboratory; (b) two microcomputers with
p-inters and software; (c) about 2,000 textbooks and a collection of
videocassettes; and (d) provision for maintenance of equipment and
materials provided.
12.       Coordination and Management.  The implementation and daily
management of this project component will be the responsibility of the MEN
in cooperation with ISS and ENS management.
13.       Costs.  Summary information of costs and the phasing of
expenditures are provided in Table 1. Annex 3-18 provides further
information on the civil works to be undertaken to install the ISS in
Rosso. As regards the laboratory equipment, preliminary lists are
available; these need to be updated in connection with the preparation of
bidding documents.
