ISLAMIC REPUBLIC OF MAURITANIA
EDUCATION SECTOR RESTRUCTURING PROJECT
RestructuiiJg of Technical Education
1.        Background.  The provision of technical secondary education in
Mauritania started in 1964 when the Lyc4e/Coll&ge d'Enseignement Technique
Industriel (LCET) was established in Nouakchott. This school, which was
originally designed for 600 students along the traditional lines of the
French education system, has since expanded to its present enrollment of
1069 students taking courses in general mechanics, electric technology,
fabrication, welding, and automotive repair. In 1984, an additional
Collage d'Enseignement Technique (CET - Mamadou Tour#) was established in
Nouadhibou to focus on vocational education for the commercial fisheries
sector. The school had a 1987 enrollment of 94 students taking courses in
industrial refrigeration and marine diesel mechanics. Both schools are
administered by the Directorate of Technical Education (DET) under the
Ministry of Education (MEN).
2.        Despite extensive foreign assistance (primarily from FAC) over
the years, technical education hap not been sufficiently responsive to
labor market demands, nor has it been effective in preparing young people
for career occupations. The major factors affecting this condition have
been identified as follows:
(a) During the ten year period between 1976 and 1986, approximately 7,900
students (less than 4Z) were enrolled in technical education programs
out of a total secondary enrollment of 215,000. This figure is highly
disproportionate to the needs of the labor market for workers with
technical skill qualifications.
(b) Technical education programs have focussed primarily on heavy industry
occupations which: (i) tended to duplicate training efforts done
within the industry (most notably SNIM); (ii) did not cover other
areas of the labor market; and (iii) have not been able to adapt to
changes in the economy.
(c) Technical education programs tend to be more theoretical than
practical which hinders the proper acquisition of job skills required
in the labor market. Within the present 36-hour school week, only 14
hours are spent on technical studies and on acquiring practical
experience. This problem is often due to the lack of workshop
equipment and materials, the lack of technical teacher skills and
experience, and teacher preference for academic approaches as a means
to overcome the two previously-mentioned factors.
(d) Technical education is more often utilized by students to provide
access to higher education, than to provide access to the labor
market. This subversion of technical education objectives results in
high costs to achieve results that could be obtained in general
education programs while, at the same time, little is achieved in
improving the skills available within the work force. It should also



- 98 -
ANNEX 3-9
Page 2 of 9
be noted that most of these students would have remained in general
secondary schools (at a much lower unit cost) had they been able to
gain admission.
(e) Under the present system, students enter the technical colleges after
completing primary education. At this stage of their education, they
frequently lack sufficient general knowledge to enable them to absorb
the complexities of technical subject matter and skill development.
In addition, at the age of about 12, students are often physically
underdeveloped fcr the rigors of the workshop. As a result, students
presently follow a three-year program with a higher than normal
percentage of general education subjects, in part to help overcome
these deficiencies.
(f) Under the present system, no programs are offered in the commercial
areas (i.e., secretarial, accounting, and marketing occupations) which
partly explains the all male student enrollment in secondary technical
education. In addition, the present system does not offer programs in
agriculture mechanics.
The above factors have resulted in the poor quality of technical
instruction, a poor response to labor market demands, and most of all, a
poor response to the career needs of students.
3.        The MEN also provides post-secondary technical programs for high
level technicians (techniciens superieurs) and technical teachers. In
response to a demand from the mining sector (SNIM) and the fact that almost
all technical instructors are expatriates, the Centre Superieur
d'Enseignement Technique (CSET) was established in 1981 on the campus of
the LCET in Nouakchott. The CSET curriculum includes two-year programs
(BTS) in electrical and mechanical technology, mechanical fabrication, and
mechanical construction. It also includes a third year of study (involving
fellowships at foreign technical institutes) for BTS graduates who wish to
become technical teachers (professeur d'enseignement technique - PET).
During the period 1981 through 1986, CSET enrolled 100 students of which 86
received their BTS diplomas (including 23 PET graduates). With the first
group of PET students finishing in 1986, initial evaluations of the PET
program indicate poor results regarding their ability to handle the
advanced studies at the foreign technical institutes, and there is no
indication that they will be able to effective'y replace expatriate
technical teachers in the foreseeable future. At present, 120 teachers are
employed in technical education of which 39 are Mauritanian delivering
non-technical courses, while the remainder are expatriates teaching the
technical subjects. This situation works to keep unit costs of technical
education much higher than if these teaching positions were held by
Mauritanian personnel.
4.        The general condition and adequacy of physical plants and
equipment in the technical education sub-sector vary from acceptable to
poor. The buildings and campus of the LCET/CSET in Nouakchott are in
reasonably good condition with adequate space for the present programs.
Much of the equipment is in good condition, although some items are
inoperable and need to be replaced. The CET-Mamadou Tour6 buildings and



- 99 -
ANNEX 3-9
Page 3 of 9
campus in Nouadhibou are in poor condition, and are in need of repair and
renovation. Educational equipment, although old and obsolete, is kept in
reasonably good operating cor.dition. All three schools experience
shortages of consumable training materials, supplies, and spare parts.
5.        Strategy.  As part of the o erall strategy to restructure the
education sector, the Government plaxs to strengthen vocational/technical
education (VTE) so that it is more responsive to the needs of the labor
market, is more focussed on developing employable skills, and is
administered on a more cost-effective basis. To this end, the Government
proposes to:
(a) increase the involvement of employers in developing VTE policies,
curricula, and evaluation criteria;
(b) diversify and expand VTE programs to include building construction,
commercial, agricultural mechanics, and commercial fishing
occupations;
(c) modify VTE curricula and improve the ratio between practical
activities and theoretical studies to emphasize the development of
employable skills and knowledge;
(d) raise grade level entry requirements to: (i) recruit more mature
students; (ii) permit a reduction of general education subjects given
in VTE programs; and (iii) reduce overall program duration;
(e) improve the staffing and administration of VTE as Legards teacher
competency, teacher/support staff ratios, and the eventual
nationalization of all technical teaching positions; and
(f) strengthen the means to implement the above strategies and improve the
quality of instruction through improved teacher training, and better
facilities and equipment to meet the objectives of the various VTE
programs.
6.        Obiectives.  In order to correct the present inadequacies in VTE
and to implement the above-mentioned strategies, the Government has
identified a series of objectives to be achieved under the project. These
include:
(a) the establishment of a VTE Advisory Council composed of members of
Government (including Ministries and parastatals), employers, and
labor organizations;
(b) the establishment of a new VTE structure within the education system
(cf. Annex 2-1) that will: (i) raise the technical school entry level
(by three years) from completion of primary education to completion of
lower secondary education; (ii) reduce general education subjects
within the technical curricula; and (iii) provide two cycles (two
years each) of VTE at tUe upper secondary level. The Colleges and the
Lycee will henceforth be called Collages and Lycee d'Enseignement
Professionnel (CEP and LEP, respectively).



- 100 -
ANNEX 3-9
Page 4 of 9
(c) a 601 increase in the annual number of VTE graduates (from about 400
to 650) with no more than a 20Z increase in recurrent costs;
(d) a 100? increase in the annual number of BTS and technical teacher
graduates (from about 50 to 100) with no more than a 30? increase in
recurrent costs;
(e) the modification of existing curricula to increase vocational and
technical subject matters by about 252, and the development of new
programs to respond to labor market demand in potential growth sectors
of the economy; and
(f) the establishment of: (i) new courses in small appliances repair and
in building trades at the LCEP-Nouakchott; (ii) a new course in metal
boat repair at the CEP in Nouadhibou; (iii) an agricultural mechanics
CEP to be located in Boghe; and (iv) a commercial LCEP to be located
in Nouakchott.
7.        Table 1 at the end of this Annex shows the effects on enrollment
in VTE after implementation of the above strategy, while Table 2 shows the
effects on administrative, teaching and support staff, and recurrent
expenditures.
8.        Policy Measures.  In order to meet the objectives of the project,
the Government will: establish an Advisory Council for VTE by the
beginning of the 1989-90 school year. The Council will include
representatives of: (i) the Ministries of Education, Economy and Finance,
Civil Service and Labor, Equipment, Mines and Industries, and Fisheries;
(ii) employers and workers associations; and (iii) other training
institutions (i.e., CFPP-Nouakchott, and CFPM-Nouadhibou). The Council
will meet at least twice each year and will establish working committees to
deal with issues concerning VTE policies, labor market needs, replacement
of expatriates, general direction and content of curricula, administration,
and budgetary requirements. Furthermore, the Government will establish the
new structure for VTE by the beginning of the 1989-90 school year. This
structure will replace the present system with two cycles (two years each)
at the upper secondary level leading to Capacite d'Aptitude Professionnel
(CAP) and Baccalaurhat Technicien (BT) diplomas, respectively.
9.        Academic Actions.  In order to provide a better response to labor
market demands, and to improve the quality of VTE programs, the MEN will:
(a) complete curricula modifications for existing VTE programs in line
with the restructuring of the system and the increase in the ratio of
professional subject matter to general subject matter;
(b) prepare curricula for the new programs for the CEP in Nouadhibou
(metal boat repair) and for the LCEP in Nouakchott (building trades
and small appliance repair). These programs will be ready to admit
students by the start of the 1989-90 school year; and
(c) prepare new curricula for the commercial programs to be conducted at
the LCEP Commerce-Nouakchott, and the agricultural mechanics programs



- 101 -
ANNEX 3-9
Page 5 of 9
to be conducted at the CEP-Boghe. The former programs are to be
completed by the start of the 1989-90 school year. and the latter by
the start of the 1990-91 school year.
10.       Administrative Actions.  In order to efficiently implement
courses and programs under the new structure, and in a cost-effective
manner, the MEN will prepare a five-year staffing plan to meet the needs of
the new VTE structure and curricula. The plan will be detailed on a school
by school basis and will include targets for the Mauritanization of
technical teaching positions. This to be completed by the start of the
1989-90 school year. The MEN will also prepare a five-year financial plan
based upon the new structure and curricula including personnel costs,
educational material coets, consumable material costs, general overhead
(including maintenance) and utility costs, and related adQinistrative
costs. The plan should pay particular attention to ways of increasing cv.st
recovery from the private sector, and should be completed by the start of
the 1989-90 school year.
11.       Means.  The project would finances  (a) architectural designs and
engineering for the new CEP-Boghe; civil works to construct the school with
a target completion date prior to the start of the 1991-92 school year;
furniture, equipment, and educational materials to be procured in time to
start the 1991-92 school year; (b) renovation of the former ENFACOS
facilities to accommodate the new commercial programs. This work, along
with the procurement of furniture, equipment, and educational materials,
will be completed in time to start the 1989-90 schoc. year; tc) renovation
and expansion of the LCEP/CSEP-Nouakchott to accommodate the revised
industrial programs, and the new programs in building trades and small
appliance repair. This work, along with the procurement of furniture,
equipmert, and educational materials, will be completed in time to start
the 1990-91 school year; and (d) renovation of the CEP-Nouadhibou
facilities to accommodate the revised commercial fishing programs. This
work, along with the procurement of furniture, equipment, and educational
materials, will be completed in time to start the 1989-90 school year.
12.       The work schedule for the above tasks and activities is shown in
Table 3, while the base cost estimates are shown in Table 4. It should be
noted that the project does not finance, specialist services, fellowships,
or recurrent costs for this component.

ISLAMIC REPUBLIC OF MAURITANIA
EDUCATION SECTOR RESTRUCTURING PROJECT
Vocational Training and Upgrading Center (CFPP)
1.        Background.  Despite the efforts made in Mauritania over the past
decade to improve the qualit, and effectiveness of the work force, the
country is still a long way from having met its needs for trained labor and
remains heavily dependent upon foreign technical assistance to carry out
skilled job assignments. This situatior. is due in part to student
preferences for non-vocational/technical studies prior to employment, and
difficulties in providing sufficient amounts of practical job-related
subject matter in the technical programs provided within the formal
educational system. Thus, to reinforce its efforts to improve the quality
of the work force, the Government will expand and improve the technical
schools under the auspices of the Ministry of Education (Annex 3-9), and
strengthen specialized training centers under the auspices of various other
Ministries to meet specific sector training needs.
2.        The primary institution responsible for general post-employment
training and upgrading in Mauritania is the Centre de Formation et de
Perfectionnement Professionels (CFPP) located in Nouakchott. The CFPP,
operating under the auspices of the Ministry of Civil Service, Labor, Youth
and Sports, became operational in January 1981 with an annual enrollment
capacity of about 150 trainees. The enrollment has increased steadily to
the point where 665 trainees attended a variety of commercial and
industrial courses during the 1987 school year (see Table 1) with a high
ratio of practical to theoretical subject matter (80Z to 20X). Ihe Center
maintains close contact with the industrial and commercial sectors through
its Advisory Council (comprising members from Government, and associations
of private enterprises and workers), and through the individual employers
that sponsor trainees to the various training courses. These contacts help
ensure that training activities respond to labor market needs as closely as
possible (this is especially the case with custom-designed courses
implemented for specific employers).
3.        Although the Center's mandate is to provide training for persons
already employed, there has been a shift away from this mandate during the
past few years: the share of employed trainees declined from 802 of
enrollment in 1981, to 452 in 1987. This is partly due to a
USAID-sponsored program to develop commercial skills which has primarily
attracted non-employed trainees, and provided a large source of revenue for
the CFPP. In addition, the shift to commercial pre-employment training has
altered the enrollment structure so that about 832 of the trainees attend
commercial courses, of which about 73Z are women.
4.        Since its inception, the CFPP has been supported by two
IDA-financed projects, UNDP, USAID, and ILO technical assistance. The
results of these efforts have been positive, with a reasonably
well-equipped training facility, a completely Mauritanian faculty (17
instructors), a competency-bas.td curriculum, and generally good linkage



- 107 -
ANNEX 3-10
Page 2 of 6
with the labor market. There are some contradictions, however, concerning
the quality of training delivered and the ability of trainees to perform on
the job. For the most part, employers and trainees appear satisfied with
the quality of instruction in the various specialized courses conducted for
those already employed, while there is dissatisfaction with the results of
pre-employment training and the competence of those graduates who are not
yet in the work force.
5.        As much as about 40Z of the CFPP's recurrent operating costs
(about UM 22 million in 1987) are received through charges to employers for
custom-designed special courses (UM 3.5 million in 1987), and foreign funds
such as a three-year USAID grant for commercial courses (UM 5.0 million in
1987). Unit costs are low (about UM 30,000 per trainee/course versus about
UM 96,000 per trainee/course in the technical schools operated by the
Ministry of Education). Adjusting for the shorter CFPP course durations
(because they concentrate only on job specific course content), the CFPP
courses are about twice as cost effective as those offered at technical
schools, while at the same time they are considered more responsive to the
needs of the labor market.
6.        Strategy.  As part of their overall strategy to improve the
quality of the work force and to reduce the need for foreign technical
assistance in all areas of formal employment, the Government plarns to
strengthen the CFPP in parallel with improvements in vocational/technical
education and training in the commercial fisheries sector. This strategy
will be implemented through: (a) improvements in the CFPP curriculum that
will permit a better response to labor market needs; (b) increased efforts
to provide training and support to small- and medium-scale enterprises;
(c) improvements in the CFPP facilities and equipment that will enhance the
quality of instruction; and (d) the transfer of responsibility for
pre-employment commercial training programs from the CFPP to the LCEP
Commerce to be established in Nouakchott under the project.
7.        Obiectives.  In order to carry out the above strategy, the
Government has identified a series of objectives to be achieved under the
CFPP component of the project, including: (a) reorienting the course
marketing and trainee selection process to restore the main thrust of the
Center's training to its original purpose of upgrading skills of already
employed workers; (b) providing for an output of about 50 trainees per year
in air-conditioning and refrigeration, 50 trainees per year in industrial
electricity and electro-mechanics, 40 trainees per year in computer
programming and data processing, and 40 trainees per year in small- and
medium-scale enterprise management; and (c) improving the quality of
instruction through enhanced curricula, instructor upgrading, and facility
and equipment upgrading. These objectives will be achieved through a
series of investments and activities that will be financed and implemented
under the project.
8.        CFPP Component.  To meet the objectives of the project as stated
above, the following tasks and activities will be carried out under the
project:



- 108 -
ANNEX 3-10
Page 3 of 6
(a) Elimination of pre-employment training at CFPP by the end of school
year 1990. This will be accomplished as the USAID-sponsored
commercial program is completed and other pre-employment training
activities are phased out and/or transferred to the new commercial
lycee.
(b) Development of curricula for courses in (i) air-conditioning and
refrigeration, (ii) industrial electricity and electro-mechanics,
(iii) computer programming and data processing, and (iv) small- and
medium-scale enterprise management.
(c) Construction of two new workshops and two new classrooms for
air-conditioning/refrigeration, and industrial electricity/electro-
mechanical courses (to be completed by the end if 1989).
(d) Procurement of training equipment and educational materials for
(i) the electro-mechanical workshop, (ii) the air-conditioning and
refrigeration workshop, (iii) the commercial classrooms, (iv) the
automotive workshop, (v) the technical drawing workshop, (vi) the
plumbing workshop, (vii) the masonry workshop, and (viii) the data
processing laboratory. Equipment and materials will be procured and
installed in time to start the 1990 school year.
(e) Provision of additional training and upgrading of CFPP instructional
staff through selected participation in regional and overseas
seminars, workshops, and conferences related to training methodology,
implementation and evaluation.
(f) Provision of specialist services (12 man-months) to assist with the
development and initial implementation of courses in computer
programming, data processing, and small- and medium-scale enterprise
management. The specialists will also provide on-the-job instruction
to CFPP faculty vis-&-vis instructional techniques in delivering these
courses.
(g) Provision of two vehicles to replace existing vehicles that are no
longer serviceable.
9.        The work schedule for the above tasks is shown in Table 2, while
Table 3 shows the cost breakdown for this component.

ISLAMIC REPUBLIC OF MAURITANIA
EDUCATION SECTOR RESTRUCTURING PROJECT
Maritime Training Center (CFPM)
1.        Background.  Commercial fishing is a major source of revenue and
employment in the Mauritanian economy. During previous years, however, the
Mauritanians have not received the full benefits of commercial fishing
activities in their waters due to the influence of foreign interests and
the large percentage of expatriates working in the sector. At present, 542
(176 out of 324 vessels) of the commercial fishing fleet is owned by
Mauritanian companies, and it is estimated that out of a work force of
about 6900, only 182 of the crews on nationally-owned vessels are
Mauritanian (see Table 1). Because of this, the costs of manning
Mauritanian vessels are high, while a thriving industry offers minimal
employment to the local labor market.  In addition, the large expatriate
composition of the f1shing sector work force hinders proper surveillance
and control of the annual catch.
2.        By the end of 1987, approximately 3500 Mauritanian seamen were
registered (and by inference, qualified for sea duty) by the
Circonscription Maritime (CM). Of this group, CM claims that approximately
1500 seamen hold jobs, although of this number perhaps as many as 50Z are
paid by the vessel owners, but are not used on board the vessels. In
response to recently enacted laws requiring at least 352 Mauritanian crew
composition on each Mauritanian vessel, the Maritime Police (Brigade
Maritime) returns non-complying vessels to port where they are fined and
restrained from fishing until the proper crew complement is deployed.
However, due to limitations of the police force, many vessels are put to
sea without complying with the crew requirements. A major factor in this
situation appears to be a shortage of experienced Mauritanian captains
which causes vessel owners to employ foreign nationals (predominantly
Korean) as senior officers. Maritime tradition permits the captain to
choose his crew, which frequently leads to the selection of fellow
countrymen to complete the crew roster. Having experienced that the
foreign crews are more productive, vessel owners have been reluctant to
interfere with this arrangement, and have shown willingness to pay the
higher personnel costs that it entails. Recently however, labor strife
among the Korean seamen has caused vessel owners to reconsider the merits
of nationalizing the vessel crews, and they are now showing strong interest
to work with the Government in recruiting, training, and upgrading
Mauritanian nationals for sea duty.
3.        In addition to the seamen working on board the commercial fishing
vessels, approximately 2000 artisanal fishermen and 1600 on-shore workers
and technicians are employed in the sector. Of these, all the artisanal
fishermen and 99X of the on-shore workers are Mauritanian, which brings the
overall ratio of Mauritanians to foreigners working in the sector to
approximately 452. Regarding the status of artisanal fishermen, on-shore
enterprises that purchase the catch state that it is often necessary to
provide credit, management and technicai assistance (directly by the



- 113 -                   ANNEX 3-11
Page 2 of 9
enterprise) in order to maintain even minimal levels of productivity. This
inter-dependent relationship between the large commercial establishments
and the artisanal fishermen is maintained because of a market demand for
the types and quality of their catch which cannot be duplicated by the
larger fishing vessels.  Regarding the status of the on-shore workers and
technicians, there is general satisfaction with the quality of work output
and production efficiency.
4.        In 1978, having recognized the need to increase both the quantity
and quality of the fishing sector work force, the Government legally
established the Centre de Formation Professionnelle Maritime (CFPM) in
Nouadhibou, the main center of activity in the commercial fishing sector.
The Center's mandate was to provide maritime pre-employment training at the
CAP (Capacit4 d'Aptitude Professionnelle) level and upgrading (recyclage)
for registered seamen (deck hands, refrigeration mechanics, and engine room
mechanics). Due to delays in implementation, the Center did not begin
training operations until 1981, and by 1987, had upgraded approximately 600
seamen (deck hands only). Due to a lack of qualified instructors and
appropriate training equipment, training has not been implemenced in
refrigeration and engine room mechanics in the CAP program. Under the
guidance of a governing board comprising members of government, commercial
fishing enterprises, and workers organizations, the CFPM presently operates
with an annual budget of approximately UM 24.0 million. This budget is
provided through the Government's general fund which is supported
indirectly, and in part, by a national apprenticeship tax.
5.        In an effort to strengthen CFPM operations, the Government sought
financing from IDA and the EEC under the Second Education Project. This
project component was never realized, however, due to lengthy delays in
approval and eventual withdrawal of cofinancing by EEC, and general
concerns regarding the desirability of constructing new facilities while
existing training facilities (belonging to SNIM) were underutilized in the
Nouadhibou area. As a result, while present estimates indicate the need to
train and upgrade approximately 6000 workers in all categories of
commercial fisbing sector occupations during the next 10 to 15 years, CFPM
operations have not improved and they continue to provide only a small
portion of the training needs required by the sector.
6.        Strategy.  As part of its overall strategy to improve work force
productivity and reduce the need for foreign workers in the commercial
fishing sector, the Government plans to strengthen sector training
operations in close cooperation with the employers and workers in the
sector. This strategy will be implemented through: (a) expansion of
training programs to include all job categories required for on-board and
on-shore job occupations; (b) increased emphasis on practical training,
including shipboard training exercises on training vessels, dtty tours on
commercial fishing vessels, and work attachments with on-shore enterprises;
(c) improved on-shore training facilities; (d) improved cooperation between
Government-managed training operations and the industry, including
increased financial support from commercial fishing enterprises; and



- 114 -                    ANNEX 3-11
Page 3 of 9
(e) increased efforts to maintain compliance with the present 35S
Mauritanian crew composition regulations, and the gradual increase of these
same requirements.
7.        Obiectives.  In order to cerry out the above strategy, the
Government has identified a series of objectives to be achieved under the
CFPM component of the project, including:
(a) an increase in sector training capability through improvements in (i)
training program content, (ii) facilities and equipment, (iii)
instructor competency, (iv) cooperation within the sector, and (v) the
financing of training operations;
(b) the replacement of approximately 700 foreign officers to meet
anticipated officer needs. Pre-officer training will be provided
in-country for candidates selected for overseas officer training
courses. This program is expected to achieve an annual enrollment of
85 officer candidates (50 deck officers and 35 officers/mechanics);
(c) the replacement of approximately 1200 foreign deck hands to meet
anticipated deck crew needs. Training will be provided for new
persons entering the sector work force, and for existing workers who
need upgrading. This program is expected to achieve an annual
enrollment of 50 students for training (CAP) and 120 registered deck
hands for upgrading;
(d) the replacement of approximately 200 foreign machinists to meet
anticipated engine room needs. Training will be provided for new
persons entering the sector work force in a program that is expected
to achieve an annual enrollment of 50 students (CAP); and
(e) the replacement of approximately 30 foreign refrigeration mechanics
and electricians, and the provision of additional technicians for
on-shore enterprises. Training will be provided for new persons
entering the work force in a program that is expected to achieve an
annual enrollment of ten students (CAP).
8.        Policy Measures.  In order to meet the objectives of the project,
the Government will establish an Advisory Committee on the Use of Human
Resources in the Fisheries Sector by the end of 1988. The Committee will
be set up by the Department of Human Resources in the Ministry of
Fisheries, to ensure regular communication among the principal trainers and
the principal users of human resources in the fisheries sector. The
Committee will meet semi-annually and will include high-level
representatives of the following: (i) the Ministry of Fisheries and
Maritime Economy, Department of Training and Human Resources; (ii) labor
organizations; (iii) training institutions - CFPM; (iv) private sector
companies and associations. The main objectives of the Committee will be:
(a) to identify the constraints on employment of Mauritanians in the
fisheries sector, including training, regulations, methods of recruitment,
terms of contracts, and communications; (b) to set objectives regarding



- 115 -                  ANNEX 3-11
Page 4 of 9
(i) placement of qualified Mauritanians (including CFP! trainees) with
private companies, both for *stages* on board vessels and for permanent
employment, and (ii) a gradual diversification of sources of expatriate
personnel and decrease in the need for such personnel; (c) to monitor
progress towards achieving the objectives set in (b) above, and report on
such progress annually, including numbers of employees, trainees, and
"stagiaires", and sources of recruitment; and (d) develop an action plan
for obtaining participation of the private sector in (i) financing
recurrent costs of post-employment training, (ii) providing support-in-kind
stages* on board vessels for CFPM trainees, and (iii) covering, along with
the Government, all operating costs of CFPM, by the fifth year of project
implementation.
9.        CFPM Component.  To meet the Government's objectives as stated
above, the following tasks and activities will be financed and implemented
under the project according to the schedule shown in Table 2:
(a)  develop course curricula for (i) officer pre-training, (ii) CAP Deck
Crew Training Program, (iii) CAP Machinist Training Program, (iv) CAP
Refrigeration Technician/Electrician Training Program, and (v) the
Deck Crew Upgrading Program. These cur.icula will be prepared by CFPM
teaching staff with the assistance of a technical advisory committee
comprised of representatives from the industri and with the help of
commercial fishing specialists employed under the project;
(b) acquire facilities at the SNIM Training Center (through lease or
transfer by Government) to be modified and/or renovated for training
needs as determined by the course curricula as noted in (a) above, and
prepare vehicle specifications for trainee transportation to the SNIM
Center, submit requests for proposals, and procure vehicles;
(c) prepare training equipment lists and specifications based upon the
above course curricula, submit requests for proposals, and procure and
install equipment at the new Training Center;
(d) prepare vessel specifications for one training trawler approximately
100 tons gross weight, 22 meters long, and with sufficient space for
18 trainees plus four instructors/officers. Submit requests for
proposals, procure, and commission vessel. Arrange for appropriate
operating insurance according to standard maritime requirements.
Arrange for docking space and/or anchorage prior to delivery of the
vessel. Prepare specifications, submit requests for proposals,
procure, and commission at least two motorized pirogues;
(e) prepare and implement a recruitment, training and upgrading program to
meet the instructional staffing needs for on-shore and on-board
programs as noted in (a) above. Prepare and implement a staffing plan
for Training Center support personnel;
(f) strengthen contacts between CFPM and commercial fishing enterprises to
(i) improve course content and relevance, (ii) develop training



- 116 -                   ANNEX 3-11
Page 5 of 9
evaluation criteria, (iii) establish cooperative on-board training
program (on privately-owned vessels) for CFPM trainees, (iv) establish
cooperative in-plant training program (in private enterprises) for
CFPM trainees, and (v) establish job placement program for CFPM
graduates;
(g)  carry out feasibility study, submit recommendations, and establish
financing plan for training in the commercial fishing sector to
include support (i) by Government (through partial utilization of tne
apprenticeship tax and other sector specific taxes), (ii) by employers
(through direct contributions and payments in kind, i.e., provision of
training stations on fishing vessels and on-shore packing plants), and
(iii) by workers associations (through partial allocation of
membership dues for training);
(h) implement training programs as defined in (a) above; and
'i) employ specialist services to astiet with the implementation of tasks
noted in (a) through (g) above. 'h? project will provide for 96
months of specialist services.
10.       Costs and Financing.  The breakdown of project component costs,
financing, and the disbursement profile are shown in Table 3. The
following points are noted concerning project cost and financing
calculations.
Total recurrent expenditures financed by the proiect over its five-year
implementation period include:
Base costs i1L UH million
personnel costs                           110.5
vehicle operating costs                     4.0
general operating costs                    15.0
training vessel operating costs            36.1
165.6
of which 80? will be financed by IDA and 20? by Government. This
percentage is an average for the five-year period 1989 through 1993.
Annual IDA support will be made on a declining basis so that the Government
will assume full financial responsibility for recurrent costs by the end of
the project. During project appraisal, it was agreed that the present
level of Government contributions (UM 24.0 million per year) would remain
constant during the first three years of the project to provide time to
increase other sources of revenue includings (i) monetary contributions
and contributions in kind (i.e., training places on board vessels and on
shore, equipment and advisory services) from commercial fishing
enterprises; (ii) contributions from workers associations;
(iii) allocations from the national apprenticeship tax; and (iv) other
forms of sector taxation that may be deemed appropriate. Consequently, for



- 117 -                  ANNEX 3-11
Page 6 of 9
the three years 1989, 1990 and 1991, it is expected that IDA will finance
the difference between actual operating costs and the about UM 24 million
provided by the Government. The remainder of the IDA contribution
(totalling UM 132.5 million over the five-year period 1989-1993) would be
disbursed during the last two years of project implementation. The actual
IDA contribution for each year would be determined after IDA's approval of
the annual operating budget for CFPM for that year.
Recurrent expenditures not financed by the proiect include (1989 through
1993):
Base costs in UM million
leasing of SNIM facilities               36.0
student support (bourses)                51.5
general maintenance costs                20.0
officer training (overseas)              21.0
general operating costs                   5.5
134.0
This brings total estimated recurrent expenditures to UM 299.6 million over
the five year period of the project. On an annual basis, these costs will
be about UN 30.0 million and UM 54.0 million for the first and second years
of the project, then reaching a level of about UM 72.0 million (not
counting for inflation) for the remainder of the project. These figures
are based upon a gradual build-up of operational activity (putting the SNIM
facilities and the training vessels in operation) during the first two
years and achieving full enrollment by the third year.
11.       The CFPM's use of the SNIM training facilities is considered an
internal government matter. At negotiations, the Goverment presented a
signed contract for the lease of these facilities.
12.       Operating cost figures for training vessels do not include
maritime insurance. It is the policy of the Government to be self-insured;
however, in the absence of a properly earmarked fund to cover maritime
hazards, it is advisable to insure the vessel according to maritime
standards. Initial indications are that appropriate insurance would cost
about UM 2.5 million per year (or UM 10.0 million for four years of
operation under the project).

ISLAMIC REPUBLIC OF MAURITANIA
EDUCATION SECTOR RESTRUCTURING PROJECT
Summary of Support to Strengthen the Planning and Management
Capacity of the Ministry of Education
1.        Obiectives.  This sub-component aims at enhancing the capacity of
the Ministry of Education to: (i) collect and analyze data and publish
education statistics, conduct studies and planning activities, and prepare
relevant technical work as background information for decision makers; and
(ii) establish a better information system on education sector personnel
(teaching and non-teaching) in order to eliminate wastage (e.g., persons
receiving salary while not in service, some being paid twice for certain
indemnities) and improve personnel management and budgeting procedures. To
this end, the project will strengthen (a) the Directorate of Planning and
Cooperation (Direction de la Planification et de la Cooperation - DPC), and
(b) the Personnel Management Unit (Direction du Personnel - DP).
StrengtheninR the Directorate of Planning and Coordination (DPC)
2.        Background.  The DPC so far has concentrated most of its
activities on the production of the Education Statistics Yearbook which is
published regularly every year. However, the data collected in certain key
areas are very limited (e.g., on higher education or on costs and financing
of education), and almost no planning activities have been undertaken.
Furthermore, the outcome of education, in terms of student acquisitions or
labor market performance, has not been analyzed in any systematic manner.
As a consequence, neither the cost nor the benefit side of education is
well documented.  The staffing of the DPC is limited both in terms of
numbers and experience in education planning.
3.        Strategy and Work Program.  The first step would be to improve
the quality and coverage of the education statistics collected, and to
computerize these statistics in order to make high quality information
available in a timely manner, as well as simplify production of the
statistical yearbook. The improved data base would provide the foundation
for real planning activities and for the realization of specific studies.
To achieve this goal would require, inter alia, that pupil registers kept
by schools be reviewed and improved; that questionnaires used for the
collection of statistics from schools be revised; and that staff at the
school and inspectorate levels be trained to maintain registers and
complete the questionnaires.
4.        A second and more difficult step would be to develop a technical
capacity to conduct studies and to plan and monitor educational
development. To help establish such a capacity, the project would initiate
a limited number of studies to be conducted by nationals, supported by
technical assistance. These studies would include: (a) the establishment
of a country-wide primary school map; (b) an evaluation of experiments in
double-shift teach4na 4n rnrimary schools; (c) a tracer study of graduates



- 122 -
ANNEX 3-12
Page 2 of 6
from technical and vocational schools; (d) an analysis of cost functions
and returns to scale in general secondary schools; (e) a study of measures
likely to promote the enrollment of girls; and (f) development and
implementation of measures to promote the provision of private education.
Such studies are of importance in improving the overall efficiency of the
school system while, at the same time, providing invaluable work experience
through which the capacity of DPC staff could be strengthened. Short
training courses, either to introduce operational planning techniques or to
assess policy implications of technical work, will complement this
learning-by-doing training strategy.
5.        The DPC is also in charge of programming and supervising school
construction. While school construction at the primary level was
previously the responsibility of both the local communities and the
Government, the full responsibility has now been passed on to the local
communities. However, the Government, although released from direct school
construction activities, still performs important functions through the
School Construction Service (SCS) of the DPC: planning construction of new
schools and maintenance of existing ones; establishment of a
community-based maintenance system; establishment of construction standards
so that efficient (i.e., low-cost and durable) construction techniques are
used; and definition of administrative and financial regulations pertaining
to the contracting out of civil works to local firms. These tasks are to
be performed at the national level, while data collection and supervision
of construction and maintenance activities will be performed at the
regional level through the staff of the Regional Primary School
Inspectorates -- Directions Regionales de l'Enseignement Fondamental (DREF)
(see Annex 3-6 for the DREFs, and Annex 3-2 for a description of the
Primary School Construction and Maintenance Program).
6.        Means.  Table 1 summarizes the support for the DPC except for
support for the School Construction Service (SCS), which is summarized in
Table 2. Support for the DPC includes: (a) 36 man-months of long-term
specialist services (educational planner) and 24 man-months of short-term
specialist services to assist in the preparation of studies and future
education prnients; (b) resources to organize two national seminars on
school mapping, five seminars (one per year) for staff of the DREFs and one
other national seminar per year on different aspects of educational
development/planning; (c) a total of 72 man-months of scholarships abroad
in the fields of educational planning, statistics and management, and a
provision for two man-months per year for participation in regional or
international seminars; (d) a lump sum for equipment; and (e) a lump sum
provision for recurrent costs. Support to the SCS includes: (i) furniture
and equipment including one vehicle; (ii) 36 man-months of long-term
specialist services (architect) and 60 man-months of locally recruited
specialist services; (iii) resources for organizing seminars related to
laur.ching SCS activities and for the transfer of school maintenance
responsibilities to local communities; and (iv) recurrent costs for office
supplies and operation of the two vehicles.
Strengthening of the Personnel Directorate (DP)
7.        Background.  The Ministry of Education employs over 7000 people
(comprising more than one-third of civil servants), and there is little



- 123 -
ANNEX 3-12
Page 3 of 6
basic documentation of the individuals' characteristics, job history, or
job location, as no records are systematically maintained. There is a
computerized file for the payment of salaries and of the various
indemnities linked to an individual's personal situation (indemnity for
teaching duty, lodging, dependents, job location, etc.). However, limited
possibilities exist for controlling the accuracy of this computerized file
due to a lack of information. Also, the responsibility for updating the
file is not clearly defined. As a consequence, Mauritanian officials
suggest that the wage bill could be inflated by as much as 5-10? due to
factors such as double payments for indemnities or payments to persons who
are no longer in service.  Considering that salaries represent about 752 of
the education budget, it is clear that readily available accurate
information on individual situations would yield substantial benefits.
Furthermore, this knowledge, if continually updated, would be of primary
importance in preparing provisional budgets to document the financial
implications of alternative potential policy options for the education
sector and to more efficiently manage the careers of this numerous
personnel.
8.        StrateRy and Work Program.  The DP will conduct a direct survey
of all employees of the Ministry of Education to establish reliable
individual records on aspects such as demographic characteristics, training
and employment records, and salaries and indemnities. The data thus
collected will be used to update existing manual records before being
computerized and compared to those available on salary payments.
Adjustments in the latter file will then be made to eliminate waste. The
manual and electronic files of the DP will be organized in such a way so as
to permit easy updating on a continuous basis as changes occur or
recruitments are made. To achieve this goal, relevant changes will be made
in the "circuit of information" within the Ministry of Education. In
particular, the present direct link between inspectors, prefets or
political power, on the one hand, and financial services on the other,
should be discontinued. All information required to update records should
be channelled through the DP, which would be given the central
responsibility as regards the updating of the payment file.
9.        Means.  The project would finance:  (a) furniture and equipment,
related particularly to the improvement of record-keeping 1/ ; (b) 12
man-months of specialist services to help develop and computerize personnel
records; (c) one local seminar per year for staff at the DREFs and 12
man-months of training abroad; and (d) recurrent costs to conduct a
complete census of all Ministry of Education staff during the first year of
project operation, cover travel costs (fuel) for field trips to update
files, and provide training. Table 3 summarizes the project's support for
the DP. Note: The computer equipment required will be provided by the
ongoing IDA-financed Development Management Project.

equipment included.

ISLAMIC REPUBLIC OF MAURITANIA
EDUCATION SECTOR RESTRUCTURING PROJECT
Support to Strengthen the Human Resources Divisions in the
Ministry of Economy and Finance and in the Ministry of Fisheries
1.        Obiectives.  This component aims at enhancing the Government's
capability to coordinate the activities of the various institutions in
charge of human resource development (schools, public and private training
centers, institutions of higher education at home and abroad), and
rationally utilize those trained (in the civil service, parastatals,
private firms and the informal sector). To this end, the project will help
to: (a) create a Human Resources Unit in the Ministry of Economy and
Finance; and (b) strengthen the newly created Directorate of Human
Resources in the Ministry of Fisheries. Outside the project, the UNDP
would establish a Manpower Planning Unit in the Ministry of Labor.
Creation of a Human Resources Unit in the Ministry of Economy and Finance
2.        Background.  The Ministry of Economy and Finance is currently
being reorganized with the assistance of the World Bank's Institutional
Development Project. As part of this reorganization, a Human Resources
Unit will be created. This Unit will coordinate data collection on various
aspects of human resources and consolidate them into an analytical
policy-oriented framework. The Unit's objective is to establish a
consistent, overall human resources development strategy and to monitor
further adjustment over time. The Unit will play a pivotal role in
determining ways through which the education and training system can
respond more adequately and efficiently to labor market demands. It will
also ensure consistency between the country's sectoral development
strategies and macroeconomic policies (development projects, labor
regulations, taxation, agreement for investment, etc.) with human resources
development so as to foster economic development, productivity gains and
job creation.
3.        Strategy.  So far, little planning has actually taken place in
the various ministries. The activities of the Directorate of Planning and
Coordination (DFC) in the Ministry of Education are largely limited to the
collection of regular school statistics (cf. Annex 3-12). There is no
regular collection and analysis of cost and financial data, and few
education and manpower planning activities have been developed. Little is
known as regards how well the education and training system responds to
labor market needs as no tracer studies exist. With regard to the labor
market, some of the most basic statistics are not collected at all, and
there is little analysis of available data. In this context, the most
urgent task is to: (i) determine an overall framework for carrying out
routine monitoring of human resource development; and (ii) initiate the
relevant data collection and analysis to be conducted. The primary role of



128 -
ANNEX 3-13
Page 2 of 3
the Human Resources Unit in the Ministry of Economy and Finance will be to
promote such studies to be carried out in relevant parts of the
administration.
4.        Means.  The lroject will finance a total of 27 man-months of
technical assistance to help establish the Human Resources Unit, initiate
studies and train Mauritanian counterparts. The technical assistance
includes a long-term umbrella contract with a foreign institution (l8
man-months spread over the first four years of the project) to render
assistance in a variety of activities within a unified approach in order to
ensure quality and continuity of the arrangement. The remaining nine
man-months of technical assistance are for recruitment of short-term
consultants. The project also provides funds with which the Unit can
contract out for inputs from other government agencies and for organization
of training activities and seminars. The project will also finance the
purchase of materials and small equipment for the Unit's operation.
Strengthening the Human Resources Directorate in the Ministry of Fisheries
5.        Background.  About two-thirds of the 6,900 persons employed in
the industrial fishing fleet operating in Mauritanian waters are
foreigners. A medium-term objective of the country is to increase the
share of Mauritanians in this occupation. To this end, the Maritime
Training Center (CFPM) (to be strengthened under the project, cf.
Annex 3-11) will train and upgrade Mauritanian seamen. More specialized
training will also be provided through studies abroad. New activities,
such as control over fishing operations and surveillance of the maritime
zone, will require the availability of qualified staff. The fact that
these are new activities accentuates the need to plan and monitor carefully
over time, and to make appropriate adjustments in, e.g., related curricula,
and the number and characteristics of trainees. Adjustments will be made
through a continuous assessment of the outcome of training activities
through their impact on Mauritanian sailors. An additional activity is to
analyze labor market conditions and the behavior of both trainees and
employers so as to assess whether or not exiating rules and regulations, as
well as incentive structures, need to be revised.
6.        Strategy and work program.  The Human Resources Directorate will
have three major tasks: (a) to assess overall training needs in the
Fishery Sector and develop strategies for how to best meet these needs;
(b) to monitor training given at the Maritime Training Center (CFPM); and
(c) to monitor training of staff in the Ministry of Fisheries in charge of
administering, controlling and monitoring the sector. As regards the CFPM,
the Directorate will carry out ex-post evaluations of training activities
so as to determine changes and adjustments to be made over times how to
better select trainees, how to better organize complementarities between
on-the-job training and training within the Center, both for upgrading
courses and pre-employment courses. To this end, tracer studies will be
organized. As regards the training of staff in the Ministry of Fisheries,
the Directorate will conduct an analysis of the content of jobs to be
performed and will determine task-related training to be undertaken.



- 129 -
ANNEX 3-13
Page 3 of 3
7.        Means.  The project will help strengthen this Directorate by
financing: (a) equipment and furniture; (b) 12 man-months of specialist
services, labor market niplyses and training of Mauritanian counterparts;
and (c) funds for the organization of national seminars on aspects related
to training and employment in the Fishery Sector.

for the two Human Rosource Units.

ISLAMIC REPUBLIC OF MAURITANIA
EDUCATION SECTOR RESTRUCTURING PROJECT
Proiect Cost and Financing
1.        Basis of Costs Estimates.  Project costs are based on February
1988 prices at appraisal. Cost estimates for primary school
construction/rehabilitation are based on the experience of similar
construction completed in the past five years and the recent prototype of a
low-cost classroom developed by UNESCO (BREDA) in Mauritania, using locally
produced gypsum. Other cost estimates for works and goods are based on
recent international tenders in Mauritania or in the region for similar
types of construction and goods with adjustment for regional cost
differences. The average cost per month of internationally recruited
services (US$12,000-US$13,200. including salary, overhead and subsistence),
locally recruited specialists and consultants (US$2,000-US$5,200),
scholarships abroad (US$2,000), and internationally recruited volunteer
specialists (US$1,600) are based on updated standards for computing budget
estimates for projects financed by UNDP and other United Nations sources.
2.        Foreign Exchange Component.  The estimated foreign exchange
component of the project is US$Z8.S million (76Z of the total project
cost), and is the result of the following estimated foreign exchange
percentages for the individual categories of expendituress
Civil works (general contracts)                     602
Civil works (school construction contracts)         602
CommunitylPrivate sector inputs                     0o
School furniture                                    50Z
Non-pedagogical materials and supplies             100?
International specialists                         100?
Local/National specialists                          o0
Training -broad                                    100o
Local training                                       02
Operating costs                                     802
3.        Customs. Duties and Taxes.  Project cost estimates do not include
duties or taxes. During negotiations, the Government will confirm tha. the
project would be exempt from taxes and duties.
4.        Contingencies Allowances.  Project cost estimates include
physical contingencies of: (a) 102 for the civil works, rarniture,
non-pedagogical equipment and pedagogical materials and supplies; and
(b) 5? for specialist services, training and operating costs. Allowances
for annual price escalations are as followst

million (82 of total base costs) and price contingencies at US$4.3 million

5.        Proiect Cost and Financina Plan.  An IDA credit of US$18.2

ANNEX 3-15
Page 1 of 8
ISLAMIC REPUBLIC OF MAURITANIA
EDUCATION SECTOR RESTRUCTURING PROJECT
Summarv of Recurrent ExPenditures and Cost Savings
Generated by the Proiect
1.        General.  'Table 1 gives rough estimates of cost savings and
incremental expenditures generated by the pro,ect. The assumptions behind
the calculations are explained below and in the notes to this table. In
interpreting the results shown in this Annex, it should be borne in mind
that, while the estimates of incremental expenditures probably reflect
quite closely the likely effects of the project on the Government's
recurrent budget, the estimates of cost savings are likely to understate to
a considerable extent the beneficial effects of the project. At least five
factors contribute to such an underestimation. First, the beneficial
effects of factors such as pedogogical equipment, textbooks, in-service
teacher training, improved planning and management, changes in curriculum
on labor productivity and labor market, impacts of training for the fishing
industry on income and foreign exchange, etc., are difficult to quantify
and have not been included. These effects are believed to be substantial.
Second, the impact of many of these factors will mainly be felt after the
completion of the project. Third, some effects, e.g., the benefits from
developing improved low-cost primary school construction techniques, will
accrue directly to local communities as the Government does not finance
school construction. Fourth, measures designed to promote private
education will allow for the achievement of higher enrollments for a given
public budget. Finally, the considerable restructuration in the use of
physical facilities promoted through the project will permit efficiency
gains and major savings on capital investments in years to come, especially
in higher education.
2.        Disregarding benefits of the above type, the estimates shown in
this Annex suggest that the project would result in net incremental
recuri:ent expenditures for the Government ranging from UH 47.6 million in
1987 to UM 152.0 million in the first year after project completion (1994).
Expressed as a percentage of the 1988 education budget, these costs
represent, respectively, a 1.4S and 4.5Z budget increase. As shown in
Table 1, the incremental costs are largely explained by the targetted
annual increase of.9S in the recurrent budget for primary education.
3.        Analysis.  The first section of the table provides estimates of
the financial impact of the measures included in the Sectoral Adjustment



- 137 -
ANNEX 3-15
Page 2 of 8
Program 1, to be implemented in the course of the project. The first four
Measures of this Program -- relating to the containment of expenditures and
enrollment in secondary and higher education -- will lead to cost savings.
In order to illustrate the order of magnitude of savings generated by the
first measure -- i.e., maintaining, at the 1987 level, scholarships in
general secondary education, teacher training and higher education -- it
has been assumed that in the absence of this measure, these scholarship
budgets would have grown at the same rate as the overall budget (32
annually). Estimated savings for Measure 2 are based on the assumption
that, in the absence of this measure, the budget would have grown at an
average of 62 per year.
4.        The savings generated by the two measures relating to
restrictions on budget growth in the first and second cycles of general
secondary education have been istimated as the difference between
continuing the budget growth experienced during the period 1981-88 (52
annually in real terms) and the growth implied by the Adjustment Program,
i.e.. 3Z for the first cycle, 22 for the first three years for the second
cycle, and 12 for the remaining period for the second cycle.
5.        The fifth Measure of the adjustment program (i.e., a 92 annual
increase in the primary education budget) would require additional
resources for primary education as compared to a continuation of past
trends in budget allocation for this level of education. The additional
resources required during the project to reach this target have been
estimated as the difference between continuing the 3Z annual increase (in
real terms) achieved during the period 1981-88 and the attainment of a 92
annual real increase. Assuming that a further reallocation of funds in
favor of primary education (other than that implied by Measures 1-4 above)
is not advisable, these additional resources would have to be made
available through an increase in total resources available to the education
sector. If the government budget were to increase annually by 32 (the
hypothesis on which these simulations are based), this would imply an
increase in education's share of the government budget, from 22.42 to 24.62
in 1993.
6.        Measure 6 (limitation of total enrollment in the ISS) would not
have any immediate cost saving effect, as present enrollment is well below
this ceiling. As regards Measure 7 (to improve the control and management
of the Ministry of Education staff to eliminate current waste), Government
officials estimate that possibly as much as 102 of the annual salary budget
for primary and secondary education is wasted through double payments of
salaries and indemnities, and through payments to people who are no longer
working in the sector. The estimated savings shown in Table 1 is based on
the hypothesis that: (a) the total waste amounts to 52 of the salary
1/ The Sectoral Adjustment Program is outlined in para. 3.2 of the report
and in Annex 3-1.



- 138 -
ANNEX 3-15
Page 3 of 8
budget; and (b) through updating and computerization of personnel records,
it would be possible to gradually eliminate such waste by the end of the
project at an accumulated rate of one percentage point per year to reach
the full 5Z saving in 1993.
7.        As regards Measure 8 (reduction of foreign teaching staff) in
1987, Mauritania had about 340 foreign teachers in all types of secondary
education (20Z of the teaching force) and 127 in higher education (492 of
the total permanent teaching force). Of these, 98 teachers in secondary
education and 84 in higher education were recruited on local contracts; the
rest were technical assistants provided through bilateral assistance. The
financial effects of a gradual Mauritanization of teachers in higher
education has been taken into account when estimating the cost savings
generated by maintaining constant the recurrent budget for higher education
(cf. para. 1 above). As regards secondary education, the approximate
comparative annual costs to Mauritania of recruiting, respectively, a
Mauritanian teacher, a technical assistant teacher and a foreign teacher
recruited on local contract, are as follows (figures in UM);
Salarl         Housing Allowance    Total
Mauritanian               357,800              60,000          417,800
Technical Assistant             0             450,000          450,000
Local contract            547,500             450,000          997,500
In view of the fact that there is a surplus of Mauritanian teachers in
general secondary education, but a shortage in technical education, the
Mauritanian Government is planning to gradually shift new technical
assistant contracts from the former to the latter type of education, and to
limit sharply the use of foreign teachers recruited under local contracts.
The cost savings shown in Table 1 assume a gradual phasing out of all 98
teachers recruited on local contracts by the end of the project , i.e.,
replacement of 20 foreigners by Mauritanian per year which yields an annual
net savings of about 20 x (997,500 - 417,800) UM - UM 11.7 million.
8.        As regards Measure 9 (to increase internal efficiency), the
project investments in factors such as textbooks, pedagogical equipment and
supplies, in-service teacher training and improved management are all
expected to improve the internal efficiency of the school system (e.g.,
reduce repetition and dropout rates and improve student achievement).
However, since these benefits are difficult to quantify, no estimates for
cost savings have been entered in the table.
9.        To the extent Measure 10 helps expand privately-financed
education, it would permit Mauritania to achieve a higher enrollment ratio
for the same level of public spending. However, no attempt has been made
to estimate possible savings.
10.       The effects of Measure 11 (increasing the contributions of
parents and local communities to school construction and training
materials) have been dealt with under point 2.1 of the table.

NOTES
1)   The annual amoritzation costs of the classrooms to be constructed
under the project are estimated to be only about half of current costs.
However, these cost savings concern the communities and have no impact on
the government budget.
2) Based on (i) one teacher per classroom, (ii) annual starting salary of
UM 268.000 and (iii) automatic promotien every two years.
3)   The operating cost of the mobile demonstration unit is borne under the
project until completion. After completion (starting in 1994) the figure
given corresponds to the annual operating cost of the unit (staff and other
operating expenditures).
4)   Since books are assumed to last three years, the figure given
corresponds to the cost to Government of producing and distributing
one-third of the stock of primary textbooks, i.e., 120,000 textbooks per
year net of the financial contribution of the user to acquire books. The
average cost of a textbook is UM 2Y0, and the users are supposed to pay UM
100 per book. The production of the initial stock of textbooks is financed
under the project. No figure is given for cost savings. However, it is
foreseen that the distribution of textbooks will result in the enhancement
of quality and, consequently, improved student achievement. These savings
are, however, difficult to evaluate quantitatively before the impact of
textbooks has been empirically assessed.
5)   The project will assist the country in conducting studies to evaluate
how double-shift teaching can be most efficiently implemented. Training of
teachers and curriculum development will be financed under the project. It
is estimated that double-shifting could be implemented in Nouakchott during
the last year of the project for grades 1 to 3 (230 classes). Class size
would be reduced from 75 to 50, and the teachers would get extra pay to
compensate for their longer school day. Later on, if the experience shows
good results, this type of teaching could be extended to other urban area
schools. The financial savings would be higher under these circumstances,
particularly since the initial class size in areas outside Nouakchott would
be lower.
6)   Buildings for regional inspectorates will be either constructed or
rehabilitated during the project. Regional inspectorates, given their new
managerial and operational tasks, will be equipped under the project so
that staff (adequate in number and qualifications) will be able to perform
efficiently. After project completion, the Government will have to bear
the operating and maintenance costs, estimated at UM 5.0 million per year.
Again, no estimate is entered for cost savings since it is difficult to
assess in quantitative terms the savings generated by improved management
of staff and better control of teaching.
7)   Science laboratories will be constructed (or renovated) and equipped,
both in secondary schools and at the Institut Superieur Scientifique. In
addition, all secondary schools will be provided with a set of minimum



- 143 -
ANNEX 3-15
Page 8 of 8
equipment to be used in regular classrooms. The investment costs as well
as the operating costs are covered by the project until 1993. When the
project is completed, the government budget will have to bear the costs of
maintenance and replacement of supplies for the effective operation of the
laboratories.
8)   120,000 science textbooks for secondary education will be produced and
distributed in the course of the project.  They are assumed to last an
average of three years. Therefore, the need for replacement is estimated
to be 40,000 textbooks per year. The production cost per book is estimated
at UM 500, and we have assumed that half of this amount could be recovered
from the sale of textbooks to users. No cost savings are shown; however,
it is expected that this investment will be very cost-effective, given the
initial scarcity of textbooks and the resulting initial imbalance in the
school input mix.
9)   The duration of vocational school programs has been reduced from three
to two years of study. Therefore, assuming that intakes are constant, this
increase would result in a reduction by one-third of the recurrent expenses
(cost savings). However, the project also provides for the renovation of
present facilities and expansion of coverage (additional areas of study in
existing schools, creation of two new schools). The investment costs are
borne by the project, while the operating costs are borne by the government
budget. The figures concerning incremental expenditures refer to the
difference between the anticipated operating costs of the system and the
cost of the previous system, assuming that the duration of vocational
programs will be revised, as mentioned above. The project incurs,
therefore, both cost savings (reduction of the duration of study) and
incremental expenditures (for the expansion of the system). The net
incremental expenditures would be the difference between incremental
expenditures and cost savings.
10) The new Centre de Formation Professionnel Maritime (CFPM) is an
extension in 'new buildings" of an existing center. The investment costs
of the creation of the new CFPM are borne by the project, while the
operating costs of the center will be borne by the government budget.
Operating costs are estimated at UM 70.0 million per year, while the
existing CFPM budget is only UM 24.0 million annually. No cost savings are
mentioned, but it is expected that the training and resulting availability
of Mauritanian nationals to work in the fishing industry will generate
income for these workers and implicit foreign currency savings for the
country.
